## Chapter: Rational functions

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on rational functions! In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of rational functions and delve into their properties, graphs, and applications. Rational functions are an essential topic in precalculus, as they provide a bridge between algebraic expressions and functions.

Rational functions are defined as the ratio of two polynomial functions. They can be expressed in the form $f(x) = \frac{p(x)}{q(x)}$, where $p(x)$ and $q(x)$ are polynomials. These functions are particularly interesting because they exhibit a wide range of behaviors and can model various real-world phenomena.

Throughout this chapter, we will cover a variety of topics related to rational functions. We will start by examining the domain and range of rational functions and discussing any restrictions on the variables. We will also explore the concept of asymptotes, which play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of rational functions.

Next, we will study the graphing techniques for rational functions. We will learn how to identify key features such as intercepts, vertical asymptotes, horizontal asymptotes, and holes. By understanding these features, we will be able to sketch accurate graphs of rational functions.

Furthermore, we will investigate the behavior of rational functions as the input approaches positive or negative infinity. This will lead us to explore the end behavior and limits of rational functions. We will also discuss the concept of horizontal asymptotes and how they can be used to determine the long-term behavior of a rational function.

Lastly, we will explore applications of rational functions in various fields such as physics, economics, and engineering. We will see how rational functions can be used to model real-world situations and make predictions.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of rational functions and be able to confidently analyze and graph them. So let's dive in and master the world of rational functions!

### Section: End behavior of rational functions

In the previous sections, we have explored various aspects of rational functions, including their domain and range, graphing techniques, and the behavior of rational functions as the input approaches positive or negative infinity. In this section, we will focus specifically on the end behavior of rational functions.

The end behavior of a rational function refers to how the function behaves as the input values approach positive or negative infinity. By understanding the end behavior, we can gain insights into the long-term behavior of the function and make predictions about its graph.

To determine the end behavior of a rational function, we need to examine the degrees of the numerator and denominator polynomials. Let's consider a rational function in the form $f(x) = \frac{p(x)}{q(x)}$, where $p(x)$ and $q(x)$ are polynomials.

1. If the degree of the numerator polynomial $p(x)$ is greater than the degree of the denominator polynomial $q(x)$, then the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of $p(x)$. 

   - If the leading term of $p(x)$ is positive, then the function will have the same behavior as a positive polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either increase without bound or decrease without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.
   
   - If the leading term of $p(x)$ is negative, then the function will have the same behavior as a negative polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either decrease without bound or increase without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.

2. If the degree of the numerator polynomial $p(x)$ is equal to the degree of the denominator polynomial $q(x)$, then the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the ratio of the leading coefficients of $p(x)$ and $q(x)$.

   - If the leading coefficients have the same sign, then the function will have the same behavior as a positive polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.
   
   - If the leading coefficients have opposite signs, then the function will have the same behavior as a negative polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity.

3. If the degree of the numerator polynomial $p(x)$ is less than the degree of the denominator polynomial $q(x)$, then the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of the denominator polynomial.

   - If the leading term of $q(x)$ is positive, then the function will approach zero as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will have a horizontal asymptote at $y = 0$.
   
   - If the leading term of $q(x)$ is negative, then the function will have the same behavior as a negative polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either decrease without bound or increase without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.

Understanding the end behavior of rational functions is crucial for analyzing their graphs and making predictions about their behavior. By considering the degrees and leading terms of the numerator and denominator polynomials, we can determine whether the function increases or decreases without bound, approaches zero, or has a horizontal asymptote.

In the next subsection, we will explore some examples to further solidify our understanding of the end behavior of rational functions.

### Section: End behavior of rational functions

In the previous sections, we have explored various aspects of rational functions, including their domain and range, graphing techniques, and the behavior of rational functions as the input approaches positive or negative infinity. In this section, we will focus specifically on the end behavior of rational functions.

The end behavior of a rational function refers to how the function behaves as the input values approach positive or negative infinity. By understanding the end behavior, we can gain insights into the long-term behavior of the function and make predictions about its graph.

To determine the end behavior of a rational function, we need to examine the degrees of the numerator and denominator polynomials. Let's consider a rational function in the form $f(x) = \frac{p(x)}{q(x)}$, where $p(x)$ and $q(x)$ are polynomials.

1. If the degree of the numerator polynomial $p(x)$ is greater than the degree of the denominator polynomial $q(x)$, then the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the leading term of $p(x)$. 

   - If the leading term of $p(x)$ is positive, then the function will have the same behavior as a positive polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either increase without bound or decrease without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.
   
   - If the leading term of $p(x)$ is negative, then the function will have the same behavior as a negative polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either decrease without bound or increase without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.

2. If the degree of the numerator polynomial $p(x)$ is equal to the degree of the denominator polynomial $q(x)$, then the end behavior of the rational function is determined by the ratio of the leading coefficients of $p(x)$ and $q(x)$.

   - If the leading coefficients have the same sign, then the function will have the same behavior as a positive polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either increase without bound or decrease without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.
   
   - If the leading coefficients have different signs, then the function will have the same behavior as a negative polynomial as $x$ approaches positive or negative infinity. This means that the graph of the function will either decrease without bound or increase without bound, depending on the sign of the leading coefficient.

Now, let's practice applying these concepts to some problems.

#### Practice problems: End behavior of rational functions

1. Determine the end behavior of the rational function $f(x) = \frac{3x^2 + 2x - 1}{x^2 + 1}$.

2. Determine the end behavior of the rational function $g(x) = \frac{4x^3 - 2x^2 + 5}{2x^3 - 3x + 1}$.

3. Determine the end behavior of the rational function $h(x) = \frac{2x^4 - 3x^2 + 1}{x^4 + 5x^2 + 2}$.

Take your time to solve these problems and check your answers. We will discuss the solutions in the next section.

### Section: Discontinuities of rational functions

In the previous section, we explored the end behavior of rational functions, which gave us insights into how the function behaves as the input values approach positive or negative infinity. Now, let's delve into another important aspect of rational functions: their discontinuities.

A discontinuity in a rational function occurs when the function is not defined at a certain point or when there is a jump or hole in the graph. These points are important to identify because they can affect the behavior and interpretation of the function.

To identify discontinuities in rational functions, we need to consider two types: removable and non-removable discontinuities.

#### Removable Discontinuities

A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, occurs when a factor in the numerator and denominator of a rational function cancels out. This cancellation results in a point where the function is undefined, but it can be "filled in" to make the function continuous.

To identify removable discontinuities, we need to factor both the numerator and denominator of the rational function. If there are common factors that cancel out, we can simplify the function by removing those factors. The point where the cancellation occurs represents the location of the removable discontinuity.

For example, consider the rational function $f(x) = \frac{(x-2)(x+3)}{(x-2)}$. In this case, we can see that the factor $(x-2)$ appears in both the numerator and denominator. By canceling out this common factor, we simplify the function to $f(x) = x+3$. The point $x=2$ represents the location of the removable discontinuity.

#### Non-removable Discontinuities

A non-removable discontinuity, also known as an essential or infinite discontinuity, occurs when the function is undefined and cannot be "filled in" to make the function continuous. These discontinuities can take the form of vertical asymptotes or holes that cannot be removed.

To identify non-removable discontinuities, we need to examine the factors in the denominator of the rational function. If there are values of $x$ that make the denominator equal to zero, those values represent the locations of non-removable discontinuities.

For example, consider the rational function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x-2}$. In this case, the denominator $(x-2)$ becomes zero when $x=2$. Therefore, $x=2$ represents the location of the non-removable discontinuity, which is a vertical asymptote.

In summary, to identify discontinuities in rational functions, we need to factor both the numerator and denominator. Removable discontinuities occur when common factors cancel out, while non-removable discontinuities occur when the denominator becomes zero. By understanding these discontinuities, we can better analyze and interpret the behavior of rational functions.

### Section: Discontinuities of rational functions

In the previous section, we explored the end behavior of rational functions, which gave us insights into how the function behaves as the input values approach positive or negative infinity. Now, let's delve into another important aspect of rational functions: their discontinuities.

A discontinuity in a rational function occurs when the function is not defined at a certain point or when there is a jump or hole in the graph. These points are important to identify because they can affect the behavior and interpretation of the function.

To identify discontinuities in rational functions, we need to consider two types: removable and non-removable discontinuities.

#### Removable Discontinuities

A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, occurs when a factor in the numerator and denominator of a rational function cancels out. This cancellation results in a point where the function is undefined, but it can be "filled in" to make the function continuous.

To identify removable discontinuities, we need to factor both the numerator and denominator of the rational function. If there are common factors that cancel out, we can simplify the function by removing those factors. The point where the cancellation occurs represents the location of the removable discontinuity.

For example, consider the rational function $f(x) = \frac{(x-2)(x+3)}{(x-2)}$. In this case, we can see that the factor $(x-2)$ appears in both the numerator and denominator. By canceling out this common factor, we simplify the function to $f(x) = x+3$. The point $x=2$ represents the location of the removable discontinuity.

#### Non-removable Discontinuities

A non-removable discontinuity, also known as an essential or infinite discontinuity, occurs when the function is undefined and cannot be "filled in" to make the function continuous. These discontinuities can take the form of vertical asymptotes or holes that cannot be removed.

To identify non-removable discontinuities, we need to examine the behavior of the function as the input values approach certain points. If the function approaches infinity or negative infinity as the input values approach a certain point, we have a vertical asymptote. On the other hand, if the function approaches a finite value as the input values approach a certain point, we have a hole.

For example, consider the rational function $g(x) = \frac{1}{x}$. As $x$ approaches $0$, the function approaches positive infinity if $x$ is positive, and negative infinity if $x$ is negative. Therefore, we have a vertical asymptote at $x=0$.

In summary, discontinuities in rational functions can be classified as removable or non-removable. Removable discontinuities occur when a factor cancels out in the numerator and denominator, while non-removable discontinuities occur when the function is undefined and cannot be "filled in" to make the function continuous. Non-removable discontinuities can take the form of vertical asymptotes or holes.

### Section: Graphs of rational functions

In the previous section, we explored the discontinuities of rational functions, which helped us understand the behavior of the function when it is not defined at certain points or when there are jumps or holes in the graph. Now, let's move on to another important topic: graphing rational functions.

Graphing rational functions allows us to visualize how the function behaves and understand its key features. By analyzing the graph, we can determine the domain and range, intercepts, asymptotes, and any other significant characteristics.

To graph a rational function, we follow a step-by-step process that involves identifying key points, determining the behavior at the asymptotes, and sketching the curve.

#### Step 1: Identify key points

The first step in graphing a rational function is to identify the key points. These points include the x-intercepts, y-intercept, and any vertical or horizontal asymptotes.

To find the x-intercepts, we set the numerator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The resulting values of x represent the x-coordinates of the x-intercepts.

To find the y-intercept, we substitute x = 0 into the rational function and solve for y. The resulting value of y represents the y-coordinate of the y-intercept.

To determine the vertical asymptotes, we set the denominator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The resulting values of x represent the vertical asymptotes.

To determine the horizontal asymptotes, we analyze the end behavior of the rational function. If the degree of the numerator is less than the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is y = 0. If the degree of the numerator is equal to the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is the ratio of the leading coefficients. If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, there is no horizontal asymptote.

#### Step 2: Determine behavior at asymptotes

Once we have identified the vertical and horizontal asymptotes, we need to determine the behavior of the rational function as it approaches these asymptotes.

If the rational function approaches a vertical asymptote from the left or right, we need to determine whether it approaches positive or negative infinity. This can be done by evaluating the function for values of x that are slightly greater or smaller than the x-values of the vertical asymptotes.

If the rational function approaches a horizontal asymptote, we need to determine whether it approaches the asymptote from above or below. This can be done by evaluating the function for large positive and negative values of x.

#### Step 3: Sketch the curve

Once we have determined the key points and the behavior at the asymptotes, we can sketch the curve of the rational function. We start by plotting the key points, including the x-intercepts, y-intercept, and any vertical asymptotes. Then, we draw the curve, making sure it approaches the asymptotes as determined in step 2.

It's important to note that graphing rational functions can be challenging, especially when dealing with complex functions. However, by following these steps and practicing with different examples, you will become more proficient in graphing rational functions.

In the next section, we will explore another important aspect of rational functions: transformations. We will learn how to transform the graph of a basic rational function and understand how different parameters affect its shape and position.

### Section: Graphs of rational functions

In the previous section, we explored the discontinuities of rational functions, which helped us understand the behavior of the function when it is not defined at certain points or when there are jumps or holes in the graph. Now, let's move on to another important topic: graphing rational functions.

Graphing rational functions allows us to visualize how the function behaves and understand its key features. By analyzing the graph, we can determine the domain and range, intercepts, asymptotes, and any other significant characteristics.

To graph a rational function, we follow a step-by-step process that involves identifying key points, determining the behavior at the asymptotes, and sketching the curve.

#### Step 1: Identify key points

The first step in graphing a rational function is to identify the key points. These points include the x-intercepts, y-intercept, and any vertical or horizontal asymptotes.

To find the x-intercepts, we set the numerator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The resulting values of x represent the x-coordinates of the x-intercepts.

To find the y-intercept, we substitute x = 0 into the rational function and solve for y. The resulting value of y represents the y-coordinate of the y-intercept.

To determine the vertical asymptotes, we set the denominator of the rational function equal to zero and solve for x. The resulting values of x represent the vertical asymptotes.

To determine the horizontal asymptotes, we analyze the end behavior of the rational function. If the degree of the numerator is less than the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is y = 0. If the degree of the numerator is equal to the degree of the denominator, the horizontal asymptote is the ratio of the leading coefficients. If the degree of the numerator is greater than the degree of the denominator, there is no horizontal asymptote.

#### Step 2: Determine behavior at asymptotes

Once we have identified the key points, we need to determine the behavior of the rational function at the asymptotes. 

Vertical asymptotes are vertical lines that the graph approaches but never touches. They occur when the denominator of the rational function is equal to zero. To determine the behavior of the function near a vertical asymptote, we evaluate the function for values of x that approach the vertical asymptote from both sides. If the function approaches positive or negative infinity, the graph will have a vertical asymptote. If the function approaches a finite value, the graph will have a hole.

Horizontal asymptotes are horizontal lines that the graph approaches as x approaches positive or negative infinity. To determine the behavior of the function near a horizontal asymptote, we evaluate the function for very large positive and negative values of x. If the function approaches a specific value as x approaches infinity or negative infinity, the graph will have a horizontal asymptote at that value.

By following these steps, we can accurately graph rational functions and gain a deeper understanding of their behavior. In the next subsection, we will explore how to interpret the graphs of rational functions and extract valuable information from them.

### Section: Modeling with rational functions

In the previous section, we learned how to graph rational functions and analyze their key features. Now, let's explore how rational functions can be used to model real-world situations.

Rational functions are powerful tools for modeling various phenomena in the real world. They can help us understand and predict the behavior of quantities that depend on one or more variables. By using rational functions, we can create mathematical models that represent real-world situations and make predictions based on those models.

One common application of rational functions is in economics. For example, rational functions can be used to model supply and demand curves in the market. The quantity demanded or supplied can be expressed as a rational function of the price, allowing economists to analyze the relationship between price and quantity.

Another application of rational functions is in physics. Rational functions can be used to model the motion of objects under the influence of forces. By considering the forces acting on an object and applying Newton's laws of motion, we can derive rational functions that describe the object's position, velocity, and acceleration as functions of time.

Rational functions can also be used in biology to model population growth. The growth of a population can often be described by a rational function that takes into account factors such as birth rate, death rate, and migration.

In engineering, rational functions are used to model electrical circuits, fluid flow, and many other systems. By analyzing the behavior of these systems using rational functions, engineers can design and optimize various processes and technologies.

When modeling with rational functions, it is important to consider the limitations and assumptions of the model. Real-world situations are often complex and may involve additional factors that cannot be captured by a simple rational function. Therefore, it is crucial to validate the model by comparing its predictions with real data and making adjustments as necessary.

In the next subsection, we will explore specific examples of real-world models that can be represented using rational functions. We will examine how rational functions can be used to solve problems and make predictions in various fields. So, let's dive deeper into the world of rational functions in real-world models.

### Section: Modeling with rational functions

In the previous section, we learned how to graph rational functions and analyze their key features. Now, let's explore how rational functions can be used to model real-world situations.

Rational functions are powerful tools for modeling various phenomena in the real world. They can help us understand and predict the behavior of quantities that depend on one or more variables. By using rational functions, we can create mathematical models that represent real-world situations and make predictions based on those models.

One common application of rational functions is in economics. For example, rational functions can be used to model supply and demand curves in the market. The quantity demanded or supplied can be expressed as a rational function of the price, allowing economists to analyze the relationship between price and quantity.

Another application of rational functions is in physics. Rational functions can be used to model the motion of objects under the influence of forces. By considering the forces acting on an object and applying Newton's laws of motion, we can derive rational functions that describe the object's position, velocity, and acceleration as functions of time.

Rational functions can also be used in biology to model population growth. The growth of a population can often be described by a rational function that takes into account factors such as birth rate, death rate, and migration.

In engineering, rational functions are used to model electrical circuits, fluid flow, and many other systems. By analyzing the behavior of these systems using rational functions, engineers can design and optimize various processes and technologies.

#### Case studies: Rational functions in action

To further illustrate the practical applications of rational functions, let's explore a few case studies where rational functions are used to model real-world phenomena.

##### Case Study 1: Supply and Demand in the Market

In economics, the concept of supply and demand is fundamental to understanding how markets function. Rational functions can be used to model the relationship between the price of a product and the quantity demanded or supplied.

For example, let's consider a simple case where the quantity demanded of a product is inversely proportional to its price. We can express this relationship using a rational function of the form:

$$
Q(p) = \frac{k}{p}
$$

where $Q(p)$ represents the quantity demanded, $p$ represents the price, and $k$ is a constant that determines the scale of the relationship.

By analyzing this rational function, economists can gain insights into how changes in price affect the quantity demanded and make predictions about market behavior.

##### Case Study 2: Projectile Motion

In physics, rational functions can be used to model the motion of projectiles under the influence of gravity. By considering the forces acting on a projectile and applying the laws of motion, we can derive rational functions that describe the projectile's position, velocity, and acceleration as functions of time.

For example, let's consider the motion of a projectile launched at an angle to the horizontal. The horizontal and vertical components of the projectile's position can be modeled using rational functions:

$$
x(t) = v_0 \cos(\theta) t
$$

$$
y(t) = v_0 \sin(\theta) t - \frac{1}{2} g t^2
$$

where $x(t)$ and $y(t)$ represent the horizontal and vertical positions of the projectile at time $t$, $v_0$ is the initial velocity, $\theta$ is the launch angle, and $g$ is the acceleration due to gravity.

By analyzing these rational functions, physicists can predict the trajectory of a projectile and understand its motion in various scenarios.

##### Case Study 3: Population Growth

In biology, rational functions can be used to model population growth. The growth of a population can often be described by a rational function that takes into account factors such as birth rate, death rate, and migration.

For example, let's consider a population that grows at a rate proportional to its current size, but is limited by a carrying capacity. We can model the population size $P(t)$ as a rational function:

$$
P(t) = \frac{K}{1 + A e^{-rt}}
$$

where $K$ represents the carrying capacity, $A$ represents the initial population size, $r$ represents the growth rate, and $t$ represents time.

By analyzing this rational function, biologists can study population dynamics, predict future population sizes, and understand the impact of various factors on population growth.

These case studies demonstrate the versatility and practicality of rational functions in modeling real-world phenomena. By understanding the underlying principles and applying mathematical techniques, we can use rational functions to gain insights, make predictions, and solve problems in various fields.

### Section: Multiplying and dividing rational expressions

In the previous section, we explored how to model real-world situations using rational functions. Now, let's dive into the topic of multiplying and dividing rational expressions. This is an important skill to master as it allows us to simplify and manipulate rational expressions, making them easier to work with.

#### Rules for multiplying rational expressions

When multiplying rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. Multiply the numerators together to get the new numerator.
2. Multiply the denominators together to get the new denominator.
3. Simplify the resulting expression, if possible, by canceling out common factors.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 1:
Simplify the expression: $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5}$

Solution:
To multiply these rational expressions, we multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5} = \frac{2 \cdot 4}{3 \cdot 5} = \frac{8}{15}$

So, $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5} = \frac{8}{15}$.

#### Rules for dividing rational expressions

When dividing rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. Keep the first rational expression as is.
2. Multiply the first rational expression by the reciprocal of the second rational expression.
3. Simplify the resulting expression, if possible, by canceling out common factors.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 2:
Simplify the expression: $\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5}$

Solution:
To divide these rational expressions, we multiply the first expression by the reciprocal of the second expression:

$\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{5}{4}$

Now, we can multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{5}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot 5}{3 \cdot 4} = \frac{10}{12}$

To simplify this expression, we can cancel out the common factor of 2:

$\frac{10}{12} = \frac{5}{6}$

So, $\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{5}{6}$.

By understanding and applying these rules, you will be able to multiply and divide rational expressions with ease. This skill is essential for simplifying complex expressions and solving equations involving rational functions. Practice these rules with various examples to strengthen your understanding.

### Section: Multiplying and dividing rational expressions

In the previous section, we explored how to model real-world situations using rational functions. Now, let's dive into the topic of multiplying and dividing rational expressions. This is an important skill to master as it allows us to simplify and manipulate rational expressions, making them easier to work with.

#### Rules for multiplying rational expressions

When multiplying rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. Multiply the numerators together to get the new numerator.
2. Multiply the denominators together to get the new denominator.
3. Simplify the resulting expression, if possible, by canceling out common factors.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 1:
Simplify the expression: $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5}$

Solution:
To multiply these rational expressions, we multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5} = \frac{2 \cdot 4}{3 \cdot 5} = \frac{8}{15}$

So, $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{4}{5} = \frac{8}{15}$.

#### Rules for dividing rational expressions

When dividing rational expressions, we can follow these rules:

1. Keep the first rational expression as is.
2. Multiply the first rational expression by the reciprocal of the second rational expression.
3. Simplify the resulting expression, if possible, by canceling out common factors.

Let's look at an example to illustrate these rules:

Example 2:
Simplify the expression: $\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5}$

Solution:
To divide these rational expressions, we multiply the first expression by the reciprocal of the second expression:

$\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{5}{4}$

Now, we can multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{5}{4} = \frac{2 \cdot 5}{3 \cdot 4} = \frac{10}{12}$

To simplify this expression, we can cancel out the common factor of 2:

$\frac{10}{12} = \frac{5}{6}$

So, $\frac{2}{3} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{5}{6}$.

#### Practice problems: Operations with rational expressions

Now that we have learned the rules for multiplying and dividing rational expressions, let's practice applying these rules to solve some problems. Try to solve the following practice problems on your own, and then check your answers with the provided solutions.

1. Simplify the expression: $\frac{3}{4} \cdot \frac{2}{7}$

2. Simplify the expression: $\frac{5}{6} \div \frac{2}{3}$

3. Simplify the expression: $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{3}{4} \div \frac{4}{5}$

4. Simplify the expression: $\frac{7}{8} \div \frac{2}{5} \cdot \frac{3}{4}$

Take your time to solve these problems, and then refer to the solutions below to check your answers.

Solution to problem 1:
To multiply these rational expressions, we multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{3}{4} \cdot \frac{2}{7} = \frac{3 \cdot 2}{4 \cdot 7} = \frac{6}{28}$

To simplify this expression, we can cancel out the common factor of 2:

$\frac{6}{28} = \frac{3}{14}$

So, $\frac{3}{4} \cdot \frac{2}{7} = \frac{3}{14}$.

Solution to problem 2:
To divide these rational expressions, we multiply the first expression by the reciprocal of the second expression:

$\frac{5}{6} \div \frac{2}{3} = \frac{5}{6} \cdot \frac{3}{2}$

Now, we can multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{5}{6} \cdot \frac{3}{2} = \frac{5 \cdot 3}{6 \cdot 2} = \frac{15}{12}$

To simplify this expression, we can cancel out the common factor of 3:

$\frac{15}{12} = \frac{5}{4}$

So, $\frac{5}{6} \div \frac{2}{3} = \frac{5}{4}$.

Solution to problem 3:
To solve this expression, we need to follow the order of operations (multiplication and division from left to right):

$\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{3}{4} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{2 \cdot 3}{3 \cdot 4} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{6}{12} \div \frac{4}{5}$

To divide these rational expressions, we multiply the first expression by the reciprocal of the second expression:

$\frac{6}{12} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{6}{12} \cdot \frac{5}{4}$

Now, we can multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{6}{12} \cdot \frac{5}{4} = \frac{6 \cdot 5}{12 \cdot 4} = \frac{30}{48}$

To simplify this expression, we can cancel out the common factor of 6:

$\frac{30}{48} = \frac{5}{8}$

So, $\frac{2}{3} \cdot \frac{3}{4} \div \frac{4}{5} = \frac{5}{8}$.

Solution to problem 4:
To solve this expression, we need to follow the order of operations (multiplication and division from left to right):

$\frac{7}{8} \div \frac{2}{5} \cdot \frac{3}{4} = \frac{7}{8} \div \frac{2}{5} \cdot \frac{3}{4} = \frac{7}{8} \cdot \frac{5}{2} \cdot \frac{3}{4}$

Now, we can multiply the numerators and denominators separately:

$\frac{7}{8} \cdot \frac{5}{2} \cdot \frac{3}{4} = \frac{7 \cdot 5 \cdot 3}{8 \cdot 2 \cdot 4} = \frac{105}{64}$

So, $\frac{7}{8} \div \frac{2}{5} \cdot \frac{3}{4} = \frac{105}{64}$.

Congratulations on completing the practice problems! Make sure to check your answers and understand the steps involved in solving each problem.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of rational functions and gained a comprehensive understanding of their properties and behaviors. We began by defining rational functions as the ratio of two polynomial functions, where the denominator is not equal to zero. We learned that rational functions can have vertical asymptotes, horizontal asymptotes, and holes in their graphs.

We discussed how to find the domain and range of a rational function by considering the restrictions on the variables and the behavior of the function at infinity. We also explored the process of simplifying and reducing rational functions to their simplest form, which allows us to analyze their characteristics more easily.

Furthermore, we examined the behavior of rational functions near their vertical asymptotes and holes. We learned that as the input approaches a vertical asymptote, the function either approaches positive or negative infinity. On the other hand, when the input approaches a hole, the function approaches a finite value.

Lastly, we discussed the process of graphing rational functions by identifying key features such as intercepts, asymptotes, and holes. We also learned how to analyze the end behavior of rational functions to determine their horizontal asymptotes.

By mastering the concepts and techniques presented in this chapter, you are now equipped to confidently work with rational functions and apply them to various real-world scenarios. The understanding gained from this chapter will serve as a solid foundation for your further studies in precalculus and beyond.

## Chapter: Conic sections

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on conic sections in "Mastering Precalculus: A Comprehensive Guide." In this chapter, we will explore the fascinating world of conic sections and their properties. Conic sections are curves that can be obtained by intersecting a cone with a plane. These curves have been studied for centuries and have numerous applications in various fields, including physics, engineering, and astronomy.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the different types of conic sections, namely the circle, ellipse, parabola, and hyperbola. We will examine their defining characteristics, equations, and geometric properties. By understanding the fundamental properties of conic sections, you will gain a deeper insight into their behavior and be able to solve problems involving these curves with confidence.

To aid in your understanding, we will provide clear explanations, examples, and illustrations. We will also discuss the connections between conic sections and other mathematical concepts, such as quadratic equations and coordinate geometry. Additionally, we will explore real-world applications of conic sections, showcasing how they are used in various fields to model and solve practical problems.

Whether you are a student studying precalculus for the first time or a seasoned mathematician looking to refresh your knowledge, this chapter will serve as a comprehensive guide to mastering conic sections. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid foundation in conic sections and be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in calculus and beyond.

Let's embark on this exciting journey into the world of conic sections and discover the beauty and power of these remarkable curves.

### Section: Introduction to conic sections

Welcome to the section on "Introduction to conic sections" in the chapter on conic sections in "Mastering Precalculus: A Comprehensive Guide." In this section, we will lay the foundation for understanding conic sections and their significance in mathematics and the real world.

Conic sections are a family of curves that can be obtained by intersecting a cone with a plane. These curves have been studied for centuries and have found applications in various fields, including physics, engineering, and astronomy. By exploring conic sections, we can gain a deeper understanding of their properties and how they relate to other mathematical concepts.

#### Understanding conic sections

To understand conic sections, let's start by examining the different types of curves that fall under this category. The four main types of conic sections are the circle, ellipse, parabola, and hyperbola. Each of these curves has unique characteristics and equations that define their shape.

- The circle is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at a right angle to its axis. It is a perfectly symmetrical curve with all points equidistant from its center. The equation of a circle can be written as $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the circle and $r$ is the radius.

- The ellipse is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at an angle that is less than a right angle to its axis. It is a closed curve with two foci, and the sum of the distances from any point on the ellipse to the two foci is constant. The equation of an ellipse can be written as $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the ellipse, and $a$ and $b$ represent the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively.

- The parabola is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone parallel to one of its generating lines. It is a U-shaped curve with a focus and a directrix. The equation of a parabola can be written as $y = ax^2 + bx + c$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants that determine the shape and position of the parabola.

- The hyperbola is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at an angle that is greater than a right angle to its axis. It is a curve with two branches that are mirror images of each other. The equation of a hyperbola can be written as $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} - \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the hyperbola, and $a$ and $b$ represent the distances from the center to the vertices.

By understanding the defining characteristics and equations of these conic sections, we can analyze their geometric properties and solve problems involving these curves. Conic sections have numerous applications in various fields, such as modeling planetary orbits, designing satellite dishes, and analyzing the trajectories of projectiles.

In the upcoming sections, we will delve deeper into each type of conic section, exploring their properties, equations, and real-world applications. We will provide clear explanations, examples, and illustrations to aid in your understanding. Additionally, we will discuss the connections between conic sections and other mathematical concepts, such as quadratic equations and coordinate geometry.

Get ready to embark on an exciting journey into the world of conic sections and discover the beauty and power of these remarkable curves. By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid foundation in conic sections and be well-equipped to tackle more advanced topics in calculus and beyond. Let's dive in!

### Section: Introduction to conic sections

Welcome to the section on "Introduction to conic sections" in the chapter on conic sections in "Mastering Precalculus: A Comprehensive Guide." In this section, we will lay the foundation for understanding conic sections and their significance in mathematics and the real world.

Conic sections are a family of curves that can be obtained by intersecting a cone with a plane. These curves have been studied for centuries and have found applications in various fields, including physics, engineering, and astronomy. By exploring conic sections, we can gain a deeper understanding of their properties and how they relate to other mathematical concepts.

#### Understanding conic sections

To understand conic sections, let's start by examining the different types of curves that fall under this category. The four main types of conic sections are the circle, ellipse, parabola, and hyperbola. Each of these curves has unique characteristics and equations that define their shape.

- The circle is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at a right angle to its axis. It is a perfectly symmetrical curve with all points equidistant from its center. The equation of a circle can be written as $(x - h)^2 + (y - k)^2 = r^2$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the circle and $r$ is the radius.

- The ellipse is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at an angle that is less than a right angle to its axis. It is a closed curve with two foci, and the sum of the distances from any point on the ellipse to the two foci is constant. The equation of an ellipse can be written as $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the ellipse, and $a$ and $b$ represent the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively.

- The parabola is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone parallel to one of its generating lines. It is a U-shaped curve with a vertex. The equation of a parabola can be written as $y = ax^2 + bx + c$, where $a$, $b$, and $c$ are constants.

- The hyperbola is a conic section that is formed when a plane intersects a cone at an angle that is greater than a right angle to its axis. It is a curve with two branches that are mirror images of each other. The equation of a hyperbola can be written as $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} - \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, where $(h, k)$ represents the center of the hyperbola, and $a$ and $b$ represent the distances from the center to the vertices along the x-axis and y-axis, respectively.

By understanding the properties and equations of these conic sections, we can solve various problems involving curves in mathematics and real-world applications. In the upcoming sections, we will delve deeper into each type of conic section, exploring their properties, equations, and applications. So let's get started on our journey to mastering conic sections!

### Section: Center and radii of an ellipse

In the previous section, we explored the different types of conic sections, including the circle, ellipse, parabola, and hyperbola. Now, let's dive deeper into the ellipse and discuss how to define its center and radii.

#### Defining the center of an ellipse

The center of an ellipse is a crucial point that helps us understand its shape and properties. The center represents the midpoint of the major axis, which is the longest diameter of the ellipse. To find the center, we need to identify the coordinates of the point where the major axis intersects the minor axis.

In the equation of an ellipse, $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, the values of $h$ and $k$ represent the coordinates of the center. The point $(h, k)$ is the center of the ellipse.

#### Understanding the radii of an ellipse

An ellipse has two radii: the semi-major axis and the semi-minor axis. These radii play a significant role in determining the size and shape of the ellipse.

The semi-major axis, denoted by $a$, represents half the length of the major axis. It extends from the center of the ellipse to the farthest point on the ellipse along the major axis.

The semi-minor axis, denoted by $b$, represents half the length of the minor axis. It extends from the center of the ellipse to the farthest point on the ellipse along the minor axis.

The lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes are crucial in calculating the eccentricity of the ellipse, which measures how elongated or circular the ellipse is.

To summarize, the center of an ellipse is the midpoint of the major axis, and the radii of the ellipse are the semi-major and semi-minor axes. Understanding these concepts will help us analyze and solve problems related to ellipses in precalculus.

In the next section, we will explore the properties and equations of ellipses in more detail.

### Section: Center and radii of an ellipse

In the previous section, we explored the different types of conic sections, including the circle, ellipse, parabola, and hyperbola. Now, let's dive deeper into the ellipse and discuss how to define its center and radii.

#### Defining the center of an ellipse

The center of an ellipse is a crucial point that helps us understand its shape and properties. The center represents the midpoint of the major axis, which is the longest diameter of the ellipse. To find the center, we need to identify the coordinates of the point where the major axis intersects the minor axis.

In the equation of an ellipse, $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, the values of $h$ and $k$ represent the coordinates of the center. The point $(h, k)$ is the center of the ellipse.

#### Understanding the radii of an ellipse

An ellipse has two radii: the semi-major axis and the semi-minor axis. These radii play a significant role in determining the size and shape of the ellipse.

The semi-major axis, denoted by $a$, represents half the length of the major axis. It extends from the center of the ellipse to the farthest point on the ellipse along the major axis.

The semi-minor axis, denoted by $b$, represents half the length of the minor axis. It extends from the center of the ellipse to the farthest point on the ellipse along the minor axis.

The lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes are crucial in calculating the eccentricity of the ellipse, which measures how elongated or circular the ellipse is.

To summarize, the center of an ellipse is the midpoint of the major axis, and the radii of the ellipse are the semi-major and semi-minor axes. Understanding these concepts will help us analyze and solve problems related to ellipses in precalculus.

### Subsection: Practice problems: Ellipses

Now that we have learned about the center and radii of an ellipse, let's practice applying this knowledge to solve problems involving ellipses. By solving these practice problems, you will gain a better understanding of how to find the center and radii of an ellipse and how to use them to analyze and solve problems.

#### Practice Problem 1:

Find the center and radii of the ellipse with the equation $\frac{(x - 2)^2}{9} + \frac{(y + 1)^2}{16} = 1$.

#### Practice Problem 2:

Given the equation of an ellipse as $\frac{(x + 3)^2}{25} + \frac{(y - 4)^2}{36} = 1$, determine the center and radii of the ellipse.

#### Practice Problem 3:

A satellite orbits the Earth in an elliptical path. The equation of the ellipse representing the satellite's orbit is $\frac{x^2}{100} + \frac{y^2}{64} = 1$. Find the center and radii of the satellite's orbit.

#### Practice Problem 4:

An artist is creating a mural in the shape of an ellipse. The equation of the ellipse is $\frac{(x - 5)^2}{16} + \frac{(y + 2)^2}{25} = 1$. Determine the center and radii of the mural.

#### Practice Problem 5:

A swimming pool has the shape of an ellipse. The equation of the ellipse representing the pool is $\frac{(x + 1)^2}{16} + \frac{(y - 3)^2}{9} = 1$. Find the center and radii of the swimming pool.

Take your time to solve these practice problems. Once you have found the center and radii of each ellipse, you can check your answers and compare them with the solutions provided in the next section.

### Section: Foci of an ellipse

In the previous section, we discussed the center and radii of an ellipse, which are essential in understanding its shape and properties. Now, let's delve into another crucial aspect of ellipses: the foci.

#### Defining the foci of an ellipse

The foci of an ellipse are two special points that play a significant role in determining its shape. They are located along the major axis, inside the ellipse, and are equidistant from the center.

To find the foci of an ellipse, we need to understand the relationship between the lengths of the major and minor axes. The distance between the center and each focus is denoted by the letter $c$.

In the equation of an ellipse, $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} + \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, the value of $c$ can be calculated using the formula $c = \sqrt{a^2 - b^2}$. This formula allows us to determine the distance between the center and each focus.

#### Understanding the significance of the foci

The foci of an ellipse help us understand its unique properties. One important property is that the sum of the distances from any point on the ellipse to the two foci is constant. This property is known as the "focus-directrix property" and is true for all points on the ellipse.

Another significant property is that the foci determine the eccentricity of the ellipse. The eccentricity, denoted by the letter $e$, measures how elongated or circular the ellipse is. It is calculated using the formula $e = \frac{c}{a}$, where $c$ is the distance between the center and each focus, and $a$ is the length of the semi-major axis.

The eccentricity ranges from 0 to 1, where 0 represents a circle and 1 represents a highly elongated ellipse. By analyzing the eccentricity, we can classify ellipses into different types based on their shapes.

#### Applying the foci of an ellipse

Understanding the foci of an ellipse is crucial in various applications. For example, in astronomy, the orbits of planets around the sun can be described as ellipses with the sun at one of the foci. This concept helps us understand the motion and behavior of celestial bodies.

In engineering and architecture, ellipses are used to design structures such as arches and bridges. The knowledge of the foci allows engineers to create stable and aesthetically pleasing designs.

In summary, the foci of an ellipse are two special points located along the major axis, inside the ellipse. They help determine the shape, eccentricity, and unique properties of the ellipse. Understanding the foci is essential in various fields, including astronomy, engineering, and architecture.

### Section: Foci of an ellipse

In the previous section, we discussed the center and radii of an ellipse, which are essential in understanding its shape and properties. Now, let's delve into another crucial aspect of ellipses: the foci.

#### Defining the foci of an ellipse

To find the foci of an ellipse, we need to understand the relationship between the lengths of the major and minor axes. The distance between the center and each focus is denoted by the letter $c$.

#### Understanding the significance of the foci

Another significant property is that the foci determine the eccentricity of the ellipse. The eccentricity, denoted by the letter $e$, measures how elongated or circular the ellipse is. #### Practice problems: Foci of an ellipse

1. Find the foci of the ellipse with the equation $\frac{(x - 2)^2}{9} + \frac{(y + 1)^2}{16} = 1$.

2. Determine the eccentricity of the ellipse with the equation $\frac{x^2}{25} + \frac{y^2}{16} = 1$.

3. A satellite orbits the Earth in an elliptical path. The distance between the center and each focus is 5000 kilometers, and the length of the semi-major axis is 8000 kilometers. Calculate the eccentricity of the satellite's orbit.

### Section: Introduction to hyperbolas

In the previous section, we explored the foci of an ellipse and their significance in understanding its shape and properties. Now, let's shift our focus to another conic section: the hyperbola.

#### Defining a hyperbola

A hyperbola is a type of conic section that consists of two distinct curves that are mirror images of each other. It is defined by the difference of the distances between any point on the hyperbola and two fixed points called the foci. Unlike an ellipse, the sum of the distances from any point on a hyperbola to the two foci is constant.

To understand the shape and properties of a hyperbola, we need to examine its equation. The general equation of a hyperbola is given by $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} - \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$. Here, the values of $h$ and $k$ represent the coordinates of the center of the hyperbola, while $a$ and $b$ represent the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively.

#### Understanding the parts of a hyperbola

Similar to an ellipse, a hyperbola has several key components that help us understand its shape and properties. Let's take a closer look at these parts:

1. Center: The center of a hyperbola is the point $(h, k)$ that represents the midpoint between the two branches of the hyperbola.

2. Vertices: The vertices of a hyperbola are the points where the hyperbola intersects its transverse axis. They are located a distance of $a$ units from the center.

3. Asymptotes: The asymptotes of a hyperbola are imaginary lines that intersect at the center of the hyperbola. They serve as guides to help us visualize the shape of the hyperbola.

4. Foci: Just like in an ellipse, a hyperbola also has two foci. These foci are located along the transverse axis, inside the hyperbola, and are equidistant from the center. The distance between the center and each focus is denoted by the letter $c$.

#### Understanding the relationship between the lengths of axes

To find the distance between the center and each focus of a hyperbola, we can use the formula $c = \sqrt{a^2 + b^2}$. This formula allows us to determine the distance between the center and each focus based on the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes.

#### Applying hyperbolas in real-life situations

Hyperbolas have various applications in fields such as physics, engineering, and astronomy. For example, in physics, hyperbolas can be used to describe the trajectory of a projectile launched with an initial velocity. In engineering, hyperbolas can be used to design antennas with specific radiation patterns. In astronomy, hyperbolas can be used to describe the paths of comets or other celestial bodies.

Understanding hyperbolas and their properties is essential for solving problems in these fields and many others. In the upcoming sections, we will explore different types of hyperbolas and their unique characteristics.

### Section: Introduction to hyperbolas

#### Subsection: Graphing hyperbolas

In the previous section, we learned about the definition and key components of a hyperbola. Now, let's delve into the process of graphing hyperbolas.

Graphing a hyperbola involves plotting points that satisfy the equation of the hyperbola and connecting them to form the curves. The general equation of a hyperbola is given by $\frac{(x - h)^2}{a^2} - \frac{(y - k)^2}{b^2} = 1$, where $h$ and $k$ represent the coordinates of the center, and $a$ and $b$ represent the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively.

To graph a hyperbola, follow these steps:

1. Identify the center: The center of the hyperbola is represented by the point $(h, k)$.

2. Determine the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes: The semi-major axis, denoted by $a$, represents half the distance between the vertices of the hyperbola. The semi-minor axis, denoted by $b$, represents half the distance between the endpoints of the conjugate axis.

3. Plot the center: Mark the point $(h, k)$ on the coordinate plane. This will serve as the midpoint between the two branches of the hyperbola.

4. Plot the vertices: The vertices of the hyperbola are located a distance of $a$ units from the center. Plot the points $(h + a, k)$ and $(h - a, k)$ on the coordinate plane.

5. Determine the foci: The foci of the hyperbola are located along the transverse axis, inside the hyperbola, and are equidistant from the center. The distance between the center and each focus is denoted by the letter $c$. To find the value of $c$, use the equation $c^2 = a^2 + b^2$. Once you have the value of $c$, plot the points $(h + c, k)$ and $(h - c, k)$ on the coordinate plane.

6. Draw the asymptotes: The asymptotes of the hyperbola are imaginary lines that intersect at the center of the hyperbola. They serve as guides to help us visualize the shape of the hyperbola. To draw the asymptotes, use the equations $y = \pm \frac{b}{a}(x - h) + k$. These lines will intersect at the center $(h, k)$.

7. Sketch the curves: Connect the points plotted in steps 4 and 5 to form the two branches of the hyperbola. The curves should be symmetric with respect to the center and approach the asymptotes as they extend outward.

By following these steps, you can graph a hyperbola and gain a visual understanding of its shape and properties. Practice graphing different hyperbolas to strengthen your skills in working with conic sections.

### Section: Foci of a hyperbola

In the previous section, we learned about the process of graphing hyperbolas. Now, let's explore the concept of foci in a hyperbola.

#### Understanding the foci of a hyperbola

The foci of a hyperbola play a crucial role in defining its shape and properties. They are two points located along the transverse axis, inside the hyperbola, and equidistant from the center. The distance between the center and each focus is denoted by the letter $c$.

To find the value of $c$, we can use the equation $c^2 = a^2 + b^2$, where $a$ and $b$ represent the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively. Once we have the value of $c$, we can plot the points $(h + c, k)$ and $(h - c, k)$ on the coordinate plane.

The foci of a hyperbola help determine the shape and orientation of its curves. They also have a significant impact on the asymptotes of the hyperbola. Asymptotes are imaginary lines that intersect at the center of the hyperbola and serve as guides to help us visualize its shape.

In the next section, we will explore the properties and characteristics of the asymptotes of a hyperbola.

Stay tuned for more insights on mastering conic sections!

#### Understanding the foci of a hyperbola

### Section: Hyperbolas not centered at the origin

In the previous section, we learned about graphing hyperbolas and understanding the concept of foci. Now, let's explore how to graph hyperbolas that are not centered at the origin.

#### Graphing hyperbolas not centered at the origin

When graphing a hyperbola that is not centered at the origin, we need to consider the coordinates of the center, denoted as $(h, k)$, and the distances of the vertices from the center, denoted as $a$ and $b$. The values of $a$ and $b$ represent the lengths of the semi-major and semi-minor axes, respectively.

To graph a hyperbola not centered at the origin, follow these steps:

1. Identify the center of the hyperbola, given by the coordinates $(h, k)$.
2. Plot the center point on the coordinate plane.
3. Determine the values of $a$ and $b$.
4. Use the values of $a$ and $b$ to find the coordinates of the vertices. The vertices are located $a$ units to the left and right of the center point.
5. Plot the vertices on the coordinate plane.
6. Find the value of $c$ using the equation $c^2 = a^2 + b^2$.
7. Plot the foci of the hyperbola. The foci are located $c$ units to the left and right of the center point.
8. Draw the asymptotes of the hyperbola. The asymptotes are imaginary lines that intersect at the center of the hyperbola and serve as guides to help us visualize its shape.
9. Sketch the curve of the hyperbola, making sure it passes through the vertices and approaches the asymptotes.

Let's practice graphing hyperbolas not centered at the origin with a few examples:

#### Practice problems: Graphing hyperbolas not centered at the origin

1. Graph the hyperbola with the equation $\frac{(x-2)^2}{16} - \frac{(y+1)^2}{9} = 1$.

2. Graph the hyperbola with the equation $\frac{(x+3)^2}{25} - \frac{(y-4)^2}{4} = 1$.

3. Graph the hyperbola with the equation $\frac{(x-1)^2}{9} - \frac{(y-2)^2}{16} = 1$.

Take your time to graph these hyperbolas and check your answers. Once you're done, you can compare your solutions with the answers provided in the next section.

Stay tuned for more insights on mastering conic sections!

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of conic sections. We began by understanding the basic definitions and properties of conic sections, including circles, ellipses, parabolas, and hyperbolas. We learned how to identify and graph these conic sections using their general equations and key features such as the center, foci, vertices, and asymptotes.

Throughout the chapter, we also delved into the algebraic representations of conic sections. We learned how to manipulate and transform their equations to obtain different forms, such as standard form, vertex form, and general form. These different forms allowed us to easily identify important characteristics of conic sections, such as their shape, orientation, and position in the coordinate plane.

Furthermore, we explored the connections between conic sections and various real-world applications. We saw how conic sections can be used to model the orbits of planets, the paths of projectiles, and the shapes of satellite dishes. By understanding the properties and equations of conic sections, we can better analyze and solve problems in these and other fields.

In conclusion, mastering the concepts and techniques of conic sections is essential for a solid foundation in precalculus. The knowledge gained in this chapter will serve as a stepping stone for further exploration of advanced topics in calculus and beyond. By understanding conic sections, we can better appreciate the beauty and elegance of mathematics and its applications in the world around us.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Find the equation of the ellipse with a major axis of length 10 and a minor axis of length 6, centered at the origin.

#### Exercise 2
Graph the parabola with the equation $y = x^2 - 4x + 3$.

#### Exercise 3
Determine the equation of the hyperbola with vertices at (-3, 0) and (3, 0), and asymptotes $y = \pm \frac{3}{2}x$.

#### Exercise 4
Given the equation of a circle as $(x - 2)^2 + (y + 1)^2 = 25$, find the center and radius of the circle.

#### Exercise 5
A satellite dish has a parabolic shape with a focus at (0, 5). Find the equation of the parabola if the vertex is at (0, 0).

These exercises will help reinforce the concepts and techniques learned in this chapter. Practice solving them to further enhance your understanding of conic sections.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the fascinating world of conic sections. We began by understanding the basic definitions and properties of conic sections, including circles, ellipses, parabolas, and hyperbolas. We learned how to identify and graph these conic sections using their general equations and key features such as the center, foci, vertices, and asymptotes.

Throughout the chapter, we also delved into the algebraic representations of conic sections. We learned how to manipulate and transform their equations to obtain different forms, such as standard form, vertex form, and general form. These different forms allowed us to easily identify important characteristics of conic sections, such as their shape, orientation, and position in the coordinate plane.

Furthermore, we explored the connections between conic sections and various real-world applications. We saw how conic sections can be used to model the orbits of planets, the paths of projectiles, and the shapes of satellite dishes. By understanding the properties and equations of conic sections, we can better analyze and solve problems in these and other fields.

In conclusion, mastering the concepts and techniques of conic sections is essential for a solid foundation in precalculus. The knowledge gained in this chapter will serve as a stepping stone for further exploration of advanced topics in calculus and beyond. By understanding conic sections, we can better appreciate the beauty and elegance of mathematics and its applications in the world around us.

Find the equation of the ellipse with a major axis of length 10 and a minor axis of length 6, centered at the origin.

#### Exercise 2
Graph the parabola with the equation $y = x^2 - 4x + 3$.

#### Exercise 3
Determine the equation of the hyperbola with vertices at (-3, 0) and (3, 0), and asymptotes $y = \pm \frac{3}{2}x$.

#### Exercise 4

#### Exercise 5
