## Chapter: Series

### Introduction

In the study of precalculus, one of the fundamental concepts that students encounter is series. A series is a sequence of numbers that are added together. It is a powerful tool that allows us to analyze and understand the behavior of various mathematical phenomena. In this chapter, we will delve into the intricacies of series and explore their properties and applications.

Throughout this chapter, we will cover a wide range of topics related to series. We will start by examining the basic concepts of series, such as terms, partial sums, and convergence. We will then explore different types of series, including arithmetic, geometric, and harmonic series, and learn how to determine their convergence or divergence.

Furthermore, we will investigate various techniques for evaluating series, such as the ratio test, the root test, and the integral test. These methods will provide us with valuable tools to determine the convergence or divergence of more complex series.

Additionally, we will explore the concept of power series, which are series that involve powers of a variable. Power series have wide-ranging applications in calculus and are essential for understanding topics such as Taylor series and Maclaurin series.

Finally, we will examine some real-world applications of series, including their use in finance, physics, and computer science. By understanding the principles and applications of series, students will gain a deeper insight into the mathematical world and develop problem-solving skills that can be applied in various fields.

In conclusion, this chapter on series is designed to provide a comprehensive understanding of this important topic in precalculus. By mastering the concepts, techniques, and applications covered in this chapter, students will be well-equipped to tackle more advanced mathematical topics and excel in their studies. So let's dive into the fascinating world of series and unlock the secrets they hold.

### Section: Geometric series

In the previous section, we explored the basic concepts of series and learned about different types of series, such as arithmetic, geometric, and harmonic series. In this section, we will focus specifically on geometric series and understand their properties and behavior.

#### Understanding geometric series

A geometric series is a series in which each term is obtained by multiplying the previous term by a constant ratio. The general form of a geometric series is:

$$
a + ar + ar^2 + ar^3 + \ldots
$$

Here, 'a' represents the first term of the series, and 'r' represents the common ratio between consecutive terms. It is important to note that the common ratio 'r' must be a non-zero number for the series to be considered geometric.

Geometric series have some interesting properties that make them useful in various mathematical applications. One of the key properties of a geometric series is that it can either converge to a finite value or diverge to infinity, depending on the value of the common ratio 'r'.

To determine whether a geometric series converges or diverges, we can use the ratio test. The ratio test states that if the absolute value of the common ratio 'r' is less than 1, the series converges. On the other hand, if the absolute value of 'r' is greater than or equal to 1, the series diverges.

When a geometric series converges, we can find its sum using the formula:

$$
S = \frac{a}{1 - r}
$$

where 'S' represents the sum of the series.

Let's consider an example to illustrate the concept of a geometric series. Suppose we have the following series:

$$
2 + 6 + 18 + 54 + \ldots
$$

In this series, the first term 'a' is 2, and the common ratio 'r' is 3. To determine whether this series converges or diverges, we can apply the ratio test. Since the absolute value of 'r' (which is 3) is greater than 1, we can conclude that the series diverges.

Geometric series have various applications in mathematics and other fields. They can be used to model exponential growth or decay, calculate compound interest, and solve problems in physics and engineering.

In the next subsection, we will explore how to find the sum of a finite geometric series and discuss some important properties of geometric series.

### Section: Geometric series

In the previous section, we explored the basic concepts of series and learned about different types of series, such as arithmetic, geometric, and harmonic series. In this section, we will focus specifically on geometric series and understand their properties and behavior.

#### Understanding geometric series

A geometric series is a series in which each term is obtained by multiplying the previous term by a constant ratio. The general form of a geometric series is:

$$
a + ar + ar^2 + ar^3 + \ldots
$$

Here, 'a' represents the first term of the series, and 'r' represents the common ratio between consecutive terms. It is important to note that the common ratio 'r' must be a non-zero number for the series to be considered geometric.

Geometric series have some interesting properties that make them useful in various mathematical applications. One of the key properties of a geometric series is that it can either converge to a finite value or diverge to infinity, depending on the value of the common ratio 'r'.

To determine whether a geometric series converges or diverges, we can use the ratio test. The ratio test states that if the absolute value of the common ratio 'r' is less than 1, the series converges. On the other hand, if the absolute value of 'r' is greater than or equal to 1, the series diverges.

When a geometric series converges, we can find its sum using the formula:

$$
S = \frac{a}{1 - r}
$$

where 'S' represents the sum of the series.

#### Summing geometric series

Now that we understand the concept of a geometric series and how to determine whether it converges or diverges, let's explore how to find the sum of a convergent geometric series.

To find the sum of a geometric series, we can use the formula mentioned earlier:

$$
S = \frac{a}{1 - r}
$$

where 'S' represents the sum of the series, 'a' represents the first term, and 'r' represents the common ratio.

Let's consider an example to illustrate the process of finding the sum of a geometric series. Suppose we have the following series:

$$
2 + 6 + 18 + 54 + \ldots
$$

In this series, the first term 'a' is 2, and the common ratio 'r' is 3. To determine whether this series converges or diverges, we can apply the ratio test. Since the absolute value of 'r' (which is 3) is greater than 1, we can conclude that the series diverges.

Since the series diverges, we cannot find a finite sum for it using the formula mentioned earlier. However, it is still valuable to understand the concept of summing geometric series for cases where the series does converge.

Summing geometric series is an important skill in mathematics and has various applications in fields such as finance, physics, and computer science. It allows us to find the total value of a series that grows or decays exponentially over time.

In the next section, we will explore more examples of geometric series and practice finding their sums. We will also discuss some real-world applications of geometric series to further understand their significance.

### Section: Geometric series with summation notation

In the previous section, we explored the concept of geometric series and learned about their properties and behavior. Now, let's delve deeper into geometric series by introducing summation notation, which provides a concise way to represent the terms of a series.

#### Summation notation for geometric series

Summation notation, also known as sigma notation, allows us to express the terms of a series in a compact form. It involves using the Greek letter sigma ($\sum$) to represent the sum of a sequence of terms. 

For a geometric series, the general form of summation notation is:

$$
\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} ar^n
$$

In this notation, the variable 'n' represents the index of the terms, starting from 0 and going to infinity. The term $ar^n$ represents each term of the series, where 'a' is the first term and 'r' is the common ratio.

Let's break down the components of this notation:

- The lower limit of the summation, 'n=0', indicates that we start summing the terms from the first term of the series.
- The upper limit of the summation, '$\infty$', signifies that we continue summing the terms indefinitely.
- The expression '$ar^n$' represents each term of the series, where 'a' is the first term and 'r' is the common ratio.
- The sigma symbol ($\sum$) indicates that we are summing the terms of the series.

Using summation notation, we can write the geometric series in a more concise and elegant way. It allows us to represent an infinite number of terms in a compact form, making it easier to work with and manipulate geometric series.

In the next section, we will explore how to evaluate the sum of a geometric series using summation notation and the formula we learned earlier.

### Section: Geometric series with summation notation

In the previous section, we explored the concept of geometric series and learned about their properties and behavior. Now, let's delve deeper into geometric series by introducing summation notation, which provides a concise way to represent the terms of a series.

#### Summation notation for geometric series

Summation notation, also known as sigma notation, allows us to express the terms of a series in a compact form. It involves using the Greek letter sigma ($\sum$) to represent the sum of a sequence of terms. 

For a geometric series, the general form of summation notation is:

$$
\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} ar^n
$$

In this notation, the variable 'n' represents the index of the terms, starting from 0 and going to infinity. The term $ar^n$ represents each term of the series, where 'a' is the first term and 'r' is the common ratio.

Let's break down the components of this notation:

- The lower limit of the summation, 'n=0', indicates that we start summing the terms from the first term of the series.
- The upper limit of the summation, '$\infty$', signifies that we continue summing the terms indefinitely.
- The expression '$ar^n$' represents each term of the series, where 'a' is the first term and 'r' is the common ratio.
- The sigma symbol ($\sum$) indicates that we are summing the terms of the series.

Using summation notation, we can write the geometric series in a more concise and elegant way. It allows us to represent an infinite number of terms in a compact form, making it easier to work with and manipulate geometric series.

Now, let's practice using summation notation for geometric series with some example problems.

#### Practice problems: Geometric series with summation notation

1. Write the following geometric series using summation notation:
   a) 2 + 6 + 18 + 54 + ...
   b) -3 + 6 - 12 + 24 - ...

2. Evaluate the following geometric series using summation notation:
   a) $\sum_{n=0}^{\infty} 4(0.5)^n$
   b) $\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} 3(-2)^n$

Take your time to solve these problems and refer back to the previous section if needed.

### Section: The binomial theorem

In the previous section, we explored the concept of geometric series and learned about their properties and behavior. Now, let's move on to another important topic in precalculus: the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem is a powerful tool that allows us to expand expressions of the form $(a + b)^n$, where 'a' and 'b' are constants and 'n' is a positive integer.

#### Understanding the binomial theorem

The binomial theorem provides a systematic way to expand binomial expressions without having to multiply them out term by term. It states that for any positive integer 'n', the expansion of $(a + b)^n$ can be written as:

$$
(a + b)^n = \binom{n}{0}a^n b^0 + \binom{n}{1}a^{n-1} b^1 + \binom{n}{2}a^{n-2} b^2 + \ldots + \binom{n}{n-1}a^1 b^{n-1} + \binom{n}{n}a^0 b^n
$$

In this expansion, the coefficients $\binom{n}{k}$ are known as binomial coefficients. They represent the number of ways to choose 'k' items from a set of 'n' items. The binomial coefficient can be calculated using the formula:

$$
\binom{n}{k} = \frac{n!}{k!(n-k)!}
$$

Let's break down the components of the binomial theorem:

- The term $\binom{n}{k}a^{n-k} b^k$ represents each term in the expansion. The binomial coefficient $\binom{n}{k}$ determines the coefficient of each term, while $a^{n-k}$ and $b^k$ represent the powers of 'a' and 'b' respectively.
- The expansion starts with the term $\binom{n}{0}a^n b^0$, where 'k' is 0 and 'n-k' is equal to 'n'. This term corresponds to the highest power of 'a' and the lowest power of 'b'.
- The expansion ends with the term $\binom{n}{n}a^0 b^n$, where 'k' is equal to 'n' and 'n-k' is 0. This term corresponds to the lowest power of 'a' and the highest power of 'b'.

The binomial theorem is a fundamental concept in algebra and has numerous applications in various fields of mathematics. It allows us to quickly expand binomial expressions and simplifies calculations involving powers and exponents.

Now, let's practice using the binomial theorem with some example problems.

#### Practice problems: Expanding binomial expressions

1. Expand the following binomial expressions using the binomial theorem:
   a) $(x + y)^3$
   b) $(2a - 3b)^4$

2. Find the coefficient of the term $a^2 b^3$ in the expansion of $(a + 2b)^5$.

3. Simplify the expression $(3x - 2y)^2$ using the binomial theorem.

Remember to use the binomial coefficient formula to calculate the coefficients of each term in the expansion.

### Section: The binomial theorem

In the previous section, we explored the concept of geometric series and learned about their properties and behavior. Now, let's move on to another important topic in precalculus: the binomial theorem. The binomial theorem is a powerful tool that allows us to expand expressions of the form $(a + b)^n$, where 'a' and 'b' are constants and 'n' is a positive integer.

#### Understanding the binomial theorem

The binomial theorem provides a systematic way to expand binomial expressions without having to multiply them out term by term. It states that for any positive integer 'n', the expansion of $(a + b)^n$ can be written as:

$$
(a + b)^n = \binom{n}{0}a^n b^0 + \binom{n}{1}a^{n-1} b^1 + \binom{n}{2}a^{n-2} b^2 + \ldots + \binom{n}{n-1}a^1 b^{n-1} + \binom{n}{n}a^0 b^n
$$

In this expansion, the coefficients $\binom{n}{k}$ are known as binomial coefficients. They represent the number of ways to choose 'k' items from a set of 'n' items. The binomial coefficient can be calculated using the formula:

$$
\binom{n}{k} = \frac{n!}{k!(n-k)!}
$$

Let's break down the components of the binomial theorem:

- The term $\binom{n}{k}a^{n-k} b^k$ represents each term in the expansion. The binomial coefficient $\binom{n}{k}$ determines the coefficient of each term, while $a^{n-k}$ and $b^k$ represent the powers of 'a' and 'b' respectively.
- The expansion starts with the term $\binom{n}{0}a^n b^0$, where 'k' is 0 and 'n-k' is equal to 'n'. This term corresponds to the highest power of 'a' and the lowest power of 'b'.
- The expansion ends with the term $\binom{n}{n}a^0 b^n$, where 'k' is equal to 'n' and 'n-k' is 0. This term corresponds to the lowest power of 'a' and the highest power of 'b'.

The binomial theorem is a fundamental concept in algebra and has numerous applications in various fields of mathematics. It allows us to quickly expand binomial expressions and simplifies calculations involving powers and exponents.

### Subsection: Applying the binomial theorem

Now that we understand the binomial theorem, let's explore how we can apply it to solve problems and simplify expressions. The binomial theorem can be used in a variety of situations, such as expanding binomial expressions, finding specific terms in an expansion, and simplifying complicated expressions.

#### Expanding binomial expressions

One of the main applications of the binomial theorem is expanding binomial expressions. By using the binomial theorem, we can quickly expand expressions like $(a + b)^n$ without having to multiply them out term by term. This can save us a lot of time and effort, especially when dealing with large exponents.

To expand a binomial expression using the binomial theorem, we follow these steps:

1. Identify the values of 'a', 'b', and 'n' in the expression $(a + b)^n$.
2. Determine the number of terms in the expansion by adding 1 to the exponent 'n'.
3. Use the binomial coefficient formula $\binom{n}{k} = \frac{n!}{k!(n-k)!}$ to calculate the coefficients of each term in the expansion.
4. Write out each term in the expansion, substituting the appropriate values for 'a', 'b', and the binomial coefficient.

Let's look at an example to see how this works in practice.

**Example:**
Expand the expression $(2x + 3y)^4$.

**Solution:**
To expand this expression, we can use the binomial theorem. Let's go through the steps:

1. Identify 'a', 'b', and 'n':
   - 'a' is 2x
   - 'b' is 3y
   - 'n' is 4

2. Determine the number of terms in the expansion:
   - The number of terms is equal to 'n' plus 1, so there will be 5 terms in the expansion.

3. Calculate the coefficients:
   - We can use the binomial coefficient formula to calculate the coefficients of each term. For example, the coefficient of the first term is $\binom{4}{0} = 1$, the coefficient of the second term is $\binom{4}{1} = 4$, and so on.

4. Write out each term in the expansion:
   - The expanded form of $(2x + 3y)^4$ is:
     $$
     (2x + 3y)^4 = 1(2x)^4 (3y)^0 + 4(2x)^3 (3y)^1 + 6(2x)^2 (3y)^2 + 4(2x)^1 (3y)^3 + 1(2x)^0 (3y)^4
     $$

By using the binomial theorem, we were able to quickly expand the binomial expression $(2x + 3y)^4$ into its individual terms. This can be especially useful when dealing with more complex expressions or higher exponents.

#### Finding specific terms in an expansion

Another application of the binomial theorem is finding specific terms in an expansion. Sometimes, we may be interested in a particular term in the expansion of a binomial expression. The binomial theorem allows us to determine the coefficient and powers of 'a' and 'b' for any term in the expansion.

To find a specific term in the expansion, we can use the following formula:

$$
\text{{Term}} = \binom{n}{k}a^{n-k} b^k
$$

In this formula, 'n' represents the exponent of the binomial expression, 'k' represents the term number (starting from 0), 'a' and 'b' are the constants in the expression, and $\binom{n}{k}$ is the binomial coefficient.

Let's work through an example to see how this formula can be applied.

**Example:**
Find the coefficient and powers of 'a' and 'b' for the third term in the expansion of $(x + 2y)^5$.

**Solution:**
To find the third term in the expansion, we can use the formula $\text{{Term}} = \binom{n}{k}a^{n-k} b^k$. Let's go through the steps:

1. Identify the values of 'n', 'k', 'a', and 'b':
   - 'n' is 5
   - 'k' is 2 (since we want the third term, which corresponds to 'k' = 2)
   - 'a' is x
   - 'b' is 2y

2. Calculate the binomial coefficient:
   - The binomial coefficient $\binom{n}{k}$ can be calculated using the formula $\binom{n}{k} = \frac{n!}{k!(n-k)!}$. In this case, $\binom{5}{2} = \frac{5!}{2!(5-2)!} = \frac{5!}{2!3!} = \frac{5 \cdot 4}{2 \cdot 1} = 10$.

3. Determine the powers of 'a' and 'b':
   - The powers of 'a' and 'b' can be determined using the formula $a^{n-k} b^k$. In this case, $a^{5-2} b^2 = a^3 b^2$.

4. Write out the third term:
   - The third term in the expansion of $(x + 2y)^5$ is:
     $$
     \text{{Term}} = \binom{5}{2}a^{5-2} b^2 = 10a^3 b^2
     $$

By using the binomial theorem, we were able to find the coefficient and powers of 'a' and 'b' for the third term in the expansion of $(x + 2y)^5$. This can be useful when we need to focus on a specific term or analyze the behavior of certain terms in the expansion.

#### Simplifying complicated expressions

The binomial theorem can also be used to simplify complicated expressions involving binomial coefficients and powers. By applying the binomial theorem, we can expand and simplify expressions to make them easier to work with.

Let's look at an example to see how this can be done.

**Example:**
Simplify the expression $\binom{4}{0} + \binom{4}{1} + \binom{4}{2} + \binom{4}{3} + \binom{4}{4}$.

**Solution:**
To simplify this expression, we can use the binomial theorem to expand each binomial coefficient. Let's go through the steps:

1. Expand each binomial coefficient:
   - $\binom{4}{0} = 1$
   - $\binom{4}{1} = 4$
   - $\binom{4}{2} = 6$
   - $\binom{4}{3} = 4$
   - $\binom{4}{4} = 1$

2. Simplify the expression:
   - $\binom{4}{0} + \binom{4}{1} + \binom{4}{2} + \binom{4}{3} + \binom{4}{4} = 1 + 4 + 6 + 4 + 1 = 16$

By using the binomial theorem, we were able to simplify the expression $\binom{4}{0} + \binom{4}{1} + \binom{4}{2} + \binom{4}{3} + \binom{4}{4}$ to 16. This technique can be helpful when dealing with complex expressions involving binomial coefficients.

In this section, we have explored the binomial theorem and its applications. We have learned how to expand binomial expressions, find specific terms in an expansion, and simplify complicated expressions. The binomial theorem is a powerful tool that can greatly simplify calculations involving powers and exponents, making it an essential concept to master in precalculus.

### Section: Arithmetic series

#### Subsection: Understanding arithmetic series

An arithmetic series is a sequence of numbers in which the difference between consecutive terms is constant. In other words, each term in the series is obtained by adding a fixed number, called the common difference, to the previous term.

Arithmetic series are commonly encountered in various mathematical and real-world scenarios. For example, they can be used to model the growth of populations, the accumulation of interest in financial investments, or the progression of time in a sequence.

To understand arithmetic series better, let's consider an example. Suppose we have the arithmetic series:

$$
2, 5, 8, 11, 14, \ldots
$$

In this series, the common difference is 3, as each term is obtained by adding 3 to the previous term. We can find any term in the series by using the formula:

$$
a_n = a_1 + (n-1)d
$$

where:
- $a_n$ represents the nth term of the series,
- $a_1$ is the first term of the series, and
- $d$ is the common difference.

For instance, to find the 6th term in the series above, we can substitute $n = 6$, $a_1 = 2$, and $d = 3$ into the formula:

$$
a_6 = 2 + (6-1) \cdot 3 = 2 + 15 = 17
$$

Arithmetic series also have a formula to calculate the sum of the first 'n' terms, denoted as $S_n$. The formula is:

$$
S_n = \frac{n}{2}(a_1 + a_n)
$$

where:
- $S_n$ represents the sum of the first 'n' terms,
- $a_1$ is the first term of the series,
- $a_n$ is the nth term of the series, and
- $n$ is the number of terms in the series.

For example, let's find the sum of the first 5 terms in the series above. We can substitute $n = 5$, $a_1 = 2$, and $a_n = 14$ into the formula:

$$
S_5 = \frac{5}{2}(2 + 14) = \frac{5}{2} \cdot 16 = 40
$$

Therefore, the sum of the first 5 terms in the series is 40.

Understanding arithmetic series is crucial in various mathematical concepts and applications. It allows us to analyze and predict patterns, calculate specific terms, and find the sum of a given number of terms. By mastering arithmetic series, you will develop a solid foundation for more advanced topics in mathematics.

### Section: Arithmetic series

#### Subsection: Summing arithmetic series

In the previous section, we learned about arithmetic series and how to find specific terms in the series using the formula $a_n = a_1 + (n-1)d$. Now, let's explore how to calculate the sum of the first 'n' terms in an arithmetic series.

The sum of the first 'n' terms in an arithmetic series is denoted as $S_n$. To find this sum, we can use the formula:

$$
S_n = \frac{n}{2}(a_1 + a_n)
$$

In this formula, $n$ represents the number of terms in the series, $a_1$ is the first term, and $a_n$ is the nth term. Let's understand this formula better with an example.

Suppose we have the arithmetic series:

$$
3, 7, 11, 15, 19, \ldots
$$

To find the sum of the first 6 terms in this series, we can substitute $n = 6$, $a_1 = 3$, and $a_n = 19$ into the formula:

$$
S_6 = \frac{6}{2}(3 + 19) = \frac{6}{2} \cdot 22 = 66
$$

Therefore, the sum of the first 6 terms in the series is 66.

Calculating the sum of an arithmetic series is useful in various mathematical and real-world scenarios. For example, it can help us determine the total amount of money earned from an investment with a fixed interest rate over a certain period of time. It can also be used to find the total population of a city after a specific number of years, given the growth rate.

Now that we know how to find the sum of an arithmetic series, let's practice with a few more examples to solidify our understanding.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of series in precalculus. We began by defining a series as the sum of the terms of a sequence. We then discussed the convergence and divergence of series, and introduced the important concept of the limit of a series. We learned that a series converges if its limit exists and is finite, and diverges if its limit does not exist or is infinite.

We also explored different types of series, such as arithmetic series and geometric series. We learned how to find the sum of an arithmetic series using the formula $S_n = \frac{n}{2}(a_1 + a_n)$, where $S_n$ is the sum of the first $n$ terms, $a_1$ is the first term, and $a_n$ is the $n$th term. Similarly, we learned how to find the sum of a geometric series using the formula $S_n = \frac{a_1(1 - r^n)}{1 - r}$, where $r$ is the common ratio.

Furthermore, we discussed the concept of convergence tests, which are used to determine whether a series converges or diverges. We explored several convergence tests, including the divergence test, the comparison test, the limit comparison test, the integral test, and the ratio test. These tests provide valuable tools for analyzing the convergence or divergence of series.

In conclusion, series are an important topic in precalculus that have applications in various fields of mathematics and beyond. Understanding the convergence and divergence of series, as well as the different types of series and convergence tests, is crucial for further studies in calculus and beyond.

### Exercises

#### Exercise 1
Determine whether the following series converges or diverges: $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^2}$$

#### Exercise 2
Find the sum of the arithmetic series: $$3 + 7 + 11 + \ldots + 99$$

#### Exercise 3
Find the sum of the geometric series: $$2 + 6 + 18 + \ldots + 486$$

#### Exercise 4
Use the comparison test to determine whether the following series converges or diverges: $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n^3 + 1}$$

#### Exercise 5
Use the integral test to determine whether the following series converges or diverges: $$\sum_{n=1}^{\infty} \frac{1}{n \ln n}$$

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of series in precalculus. We began by defining a series as the sum of the terms of a sequence. We then discussed the convergence and divergence of series, and introduced the important concept of the limit of a series. We learned that a series converges if its limit exists and is finite, and diverges if its limit does not exist or is infinite.

We also explored different types of series, such as arithmetic series and geometric series. We learned how to find the sum of an arithmetic series using the formula $S_n = \frac{n}{2}(a_1 + a_n)$, where $S_n$ is the sum of the first $n$ terms, $a_1$ is the first term, and $a_n$ is the $n$th term. Similarly, we learned how to find the sum of a geometric series using the formula $S_n = \frac{a_1(1 - r^n)}{1 - r}$, where $r$ is the common ratio.

Furthermore, we discussed the concept of convergence tests, which are used to determine whether a series converges or diverges. We explored several convergence tests, including the divergence test, the comparison test, the limit comparison test, the integral test, and the ratio test. These tests provide valuable tools for analyzing the convergence or divergence of series.

In conclusion, series are an important topic in precalculus that have applications in various fields of mathematics and beyond. Understanding the convergence and divergence of series, as well as the different types of series and convergence tests, is crucial for further studies in calculus and beyond.

#### Exercise 3

## Chapter: Limits and continuity

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Limits and Continuity in the book "Mastering Precalculus: A Comprehensive Guide." In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental concepts of limits and continuity and their applications in precalculus.

Limits play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of functions as they approach certain values. By studying limits, we can determine the behavior of functions at specific points or as they approach infinity or negative infinity. This knowledge is essential in various fields, including calculus, physics, and engineering.

Continuity, on the other hand, focuses on the smoothness and connectedness of functions. A function is said to be continuous if it has no abrupt changes or breaks in its graph. Understanding continuity allows us to analyze the behavior of functions over intervals and make predictions about their values.

Throughout this chapter, we will delve into the properties and techniques of limits and continuity. We will explore topics such as evaluating limits algebraically, using graphical methods to understand limits, and applying the concept of continuity to real-world scenarios. By mastering these concepts, you will develop a solid foundation for further studies in calculus and other mathematical disciplines.

So, let's embark on this journey of understanding limits and continuity. By the end of this chapter, you will have a deep understanding of these concepts and be able to apply them confidently in various mathematical and scientific contexts. Let's get started!

### Section: Defining limits and using limit notation

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of limits and their importance in understanding the behavior of functions. Now, let's dive deeper into defining limits and using limit notation.

#### Understanding limits

A limit is a fundamental concept in calculus that allows us to analyze the behavior of a function as it approaches a particular value. It helps us answer questions like "What is the value of the function as it gets closer and closer to a specific point?"

To understand limits, let's consider a function $f(x)$ and a point $a$ on its domain. We denote the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $a$ using the notation:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)
$$

This notation represents the value that $f(x)$ approaches as $x$ gets arbitrarily close to $a$. It does not necessarily represent the value of $f(x)$ at $a$ itself.

Limits can be evaluated in various ways, including algebraically, graphically, and numerically. In this section, we will focus on evaluating limits algebraically and using limit notation.

#### Evaluating limits algebraically

To evaluate a limit algebraically, we can use various techniques, such as direct substitution, factoring, rationalizing, and applying special limit rules.

1. Direct substitution: If the function is defined at the point $a$, we can simply substitute $a$ into the function and evaluate the result. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = 2x + 3$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can substitute $x = 2$ into the function to get $f(2) = 2(2) + 3 = 7$.

2. Factoring: Sometimes, factoring can help simplify the function and make it easier to evaluate the limit. For example, if we have the function $g(x) = \frac{{x^2 - 4}}{{x - 2}}$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} g(x)$, we can factor the numerator as $(x - 2)(x + 2)$ and cancel out the common factor of $(x - 2)$ to get $g(x) = x + 2$. Now, we can substitute $x = 2$ into the simplified function to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} g(x) = 2 + 2 = 4$.

3. Rationalizing: If we have a function with a radical expression in the numerator or denominator, we can use rationalizing techniques to simplify it. For example, if we have the function $h(x) = \frac{{\sqrt{x} - 1}}{{x - 1}}$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 1}} h(x)$, we can multiply the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the radical expression, which in this case is $\sqrt{x} + 1$. After simplifying, we get $h(x) = \frac{{x - 1}}{{(x - 1)(\sqrt{x} + 1)}} = \frac{1}{{\sqrt{x} + 1}}$. Now, we can substitute $x = 1$ into the simplified function to find $\lim_{{x \to 1}} h(x) = \frac{1}{{\sqrt{1} + 1}} = \frac{1}{2}$.

4. Special limit rules: There are certain special limit rules that can be applied to simplify the evaluation of limits. These rules include the limit of a constant, the limit of a sum or difference, the limit of a product, and the limit of a quotient. These rules can be used to simplify complex functions and make them easier to evaluate.

By using these techniques, we can evaluate limits algebraically and gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of functions as they approach specific values. In the next section, we will explore graphical methods for understanding limits.

### Section: Defining limits and using limit notation

In the previous section, we introduced the concept of limits and their importance in understanding the behavior of functions. Now, let's dive deeper into defining limits and using limit notation.

#### Understanding limits

A limit is a fundamental concept in calculus that allows us to analyze the behavior of a function as it approaches a particular value. It helps us answer questions like "What is the value of the function as it gets closer and closer to a specific point?"

To understand limits, let's consider a function $f(x)$ and a point $a$ on its domain. We denote the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $a$ using the notation:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)
$$

This notation represents the value that $f(x)$ approaches as $x$ gets arbitrarily close to $a$. It does not necessarily represent the value of $f(x)$ at $a$ itself.

Limits can be evaluated in various ways, including algebraically, graphically, and numerically. In this section, we will focus on evaluating limits algebraically and using limit notation.

#### Evaluating limits algebraically

To evaluate a limit algebraically, we can use various techniques, such as direct substitution, factoring, rationalizing, and applying special limit rules.

1. Direct substitution: If the function is defined at the point $a$, we can simply substitute $a$ into the function and evaluate the result. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = 2x + 3$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can substitute $x = 2$ into the function to get $f(2) = 2(2) + 3 = 7$.

2. Factoring: Sometimes, factoring can help simplify the function and make it easier to evaluate the limit. For example, if we have the function $g(x) = \frac{{x^2 - 4}}{{x - 2}}$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} g(x)$, we can factor the numerator as $(x - 2)(x + 2)$ and cancel out the common factor of $(x - 2)$ to get $g(x) = x + 2$. Now, we can substitute $x = 2$ into the simplified function to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} g(x) = 2 + 2 = 4$.

3. Rationalizing: If we have a limit involving a radical expression, we can use rationalizing techniques to simplify the expression. For example, if we have the function $h(x) = \frac{{\sqrt{x} - 2}}{{x - 4}}$ and we want to find $\lim_{{x \to 4}} h(x)$, we can multiply the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the radical expression, which is $\sqrt{x} + 2$. This simplifies the expression to $h(x) = \frac{{(\sqrt{x} - 2)(\sqrt{x} + 2)}}{{(x - 4)(\sqrt{x} + 2)}}$. Now, we can cancel out the common factor of $(\sqrt{x} - 2)$ and substitute $x = 4$ to find $\lim_{{x \to 4}} h(x) = \frac{{4 + 2}}{{4 + 2}} = 1$.

4. Applying special limit rules: There are certain rules that can be applied to evaluate limits of specific types of functions. These rules include the limit laws, the squeeze theorem, and the intermediate value theorem. We will explore these rules in more detail in later sections.

By using these techniques, we can evaluate limits algebraically and gain a deeper understanding of the behavior of functions as they approach specific values.

### Section: Estimating limit values from graphs

In the previous section, we learned about defining limits and using limit notation to analyze the behavior of functions. Now, let's explore how we can estimate limit values from graphs.

#### Estimating limits from graphs

Graphs can provide valuable insights into the behavior of functions and help us estimate limit values. By observing the graph of a function, we can make educated guesses about the value that the function approaches as it gets closer and closer to a specific point.

To estimate a limit from a graph, we look for the behavior of the function as it approaches the desired point. We pay attention to the trend of the graph and observe whether the function is approaching a specific value or diverging towards positive or negative infinity.

Let's consider an example to illustrate how we can estimate limits from graphs. Suppose we have the graph of a function $f(x)$ and we want to estimate the limit as $x$ approaches a certain value, let's say $a$. We can follow these steps:

1. Identify the point on the graph where $x$ is approaching $a$. This point is usually denoted by a circle or an open dot on the graph.

2. Observe the behavior of the function as it approaches the identified point. Pay attention to whether the function is approaching a specific value or diverging towards positive or negative infinity.

3. Based on the observed behavior, make an educated guess about the limit value. If the function is approaching a specific value, that value would be the estimated limit. If the function is diverging towards positive or negative infinity, we can indicate that the limit is either positive or negative infinity.

It's important to note that estimating limits from graphs is not always precise, but it can provide a good approximation of the limit value. To obtain a more accurate result, we can use algebraic techniques, which we will explore in later sections.

In the next section, we will delve into evaluating limits using algebraic techniques, such as direct substitution, factoring, and applying special limit rules. These techniques will allow us to find precise values for limits and further enhance our understanding of the behavior of functions.

Stay tuned for more exciting concepts in the world of limits and continuity!

### Section: Estimating limit values from tables

In the previous section, we learned how to estimate limit values from graphs. Now, let's explore another method for estimating limits using tables.

#### Estimating limits from tables

Tables can provide us with valuable information about the behavior of functions and help us estimate limit values. By examining the values of a function as the input approaches a specific value, we can make educated guesses about the limit value.

To estimate a limit from a table, we follow these steps:

1. Identify the values in the table that are approaching the desired input value. These values should be getting closer and closer to the desired input value.

2. Observe the corresponding output values in the table as the input values approach the desired value. Pay attention to whether the output values are approaching a specific number or diverging towards positive or negative infinity.

3. Based on the observed behavior, make an educated guess about the limit value. If the output values are approaching a specific number, that number would be the estimated limit. If the output values are diverging towards positive or negative infinity, we can indicate that the limit is either positive or negative infinity.

Let's consider an example to illustrate how we can estimate limits from tables. Suppose we have a table of values for a function $f(x)$ and we want to estimate the limit as $x$ approaches a certain value, let's say $a$. We can follow the steps mentioned above to make our estimation.

It's important to note that estimating limits from tables is not always precise, but it can provide a good approximation of the limit value. To obtain a more accurate result, we can use algebraic techniques, which we will explore in later sections.

### Section: Determining limits using algebraic properties of limits

In the previous section, we learned how to estimate limit values from tables. Now, let's explore another method for determining limits using algebraic properties.

#### Algebraic properties of limits

Algebraic properties of limits allow us to evaluate limits of functions by manipulating algebraic expressions. These properties are based on the fact that limits behave well with respect to algebraic operations such as addition, subtraction, multiplication, and division.

Here are some important algebraic properties of limits:

1. **Sum/Difference Rule**: If the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of their sum or difference also exists and is equal to the sum or difference of their limits. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x) \pm g(x)) = \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) \pm \lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)
$$

2. **Product Rule**: If the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of their product also exists and is equal to the product of their limits. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x) \cdot g(x)) = \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) \cdot \lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)
$$

3. **Quotient Rule**: If the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value and the limit of the denominator is not zero, then the limit of their quotient also exists and is equal to the quotient of their limits. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} \left(\frac{{f(x)}}{{g(x)}}\right) = \frac{{\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)}}{{\lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)}}
$$

4. **Power Rule**: If the limit of a function exists as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of its power also exists and is equal to the power of its limit. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x))^n = (\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x))^n
$$

5. **Constant Rule**: If $c$ is a constant, then the limit of a constant times a function is equal to the constant times the limit of the function. Mathematically, this can be expressed as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (c \cdot f(x)) = c \cdot \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)
$$

These algebraic properties of limits provide us with powerful tools to evaluate limits of functions. By applying these properties, we can simplify complex expressions and determine the limit value as $x$ approaches a specific value.

In the next section, we will explore how to apply these algebraic properties to determine limits of functions in various scenarios.

### Section: Determining limits using algebraic manipulation

In the previous section, we explored how to estimate limit values from tables. Now, let's delve into another powerful method for determining limits using algebraic manipulation.

#### Algebraic manipulation to find limits

Algebraic manipulation is a technique that allows us to evaluate limits of functions by manipulating algebraic expressions. By applying various algebraic properties, we can simplify complex expressions and determine the limit of a function as it approaches a certain value.

Here are some key algebraic properties that we can utilize to find limits:

1. **Sum/Difference Rule**: This rule states that if the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of their sum or difference also exists and is equal to the sum or difference of their limits. Mathematically, we can express this as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x) \pm g(x)) = \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) \pm \lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)
$$

2. **Product Rule**: The product rule tells us that if the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of their product also exists and is equal to the product of their limits. Mathematically, we can represent this as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x) \cdot g(x)) = \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) \cdot \lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)
$$

3. **Quotient Rule**: When the limits of two functions exist as $x$ approaches a certain value and the limit of the denominator is not zero, we can determine the limit of their quotient. The limit of the quotient is equal to the quotient of their limits. Mathematically, we can express this as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} \left(\frac{{f(x)}}{{g(x)}}\right) = \frac{{\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)}}{{\lim_{{x \to a}} g(x)}}
$$

4. **Power Rule**: The power rule states that if the limit of a function exists as $x$ approaches a certain value, then the limit of its power also exists and is equal to the power of its limit. Mathematically, we can represent this as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (f(x))^n = (\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x))^n
$$

5. **Constant Rule**: If $c$ is a constant, then the limit of a constant times a function is equal to the constant times the limit of the function. Mathematically, we can express this as:

$$
\lim_{{x \to a}} (c \cdot f(x)) = c \cdot \lim_{{x \to a}} f(x)
$$

By applying these algebraic properties, we can simplify complex expressions and determine the limit of a function with ease. In the upcoming examples, we will explore how to use algebraic manipulation to find limits in various scenarios.

Now that we have a solid understanding of algebraic manipulation to find limits, let's move on to the next section where we will explore another powerful technique: evaluating limits using L'Hôpital's rule.

### Section: Selecting procedures for determining limits

In the previous section, we learned about algebraic manipulation as a powerful method for determining limits. Now, let's explore how to select the right procedure to find limits based on the given function.

#### Choosing the right method to find limits

When faced with a limit problem, it is important to choose the appropriate method to find the limit. Different methods work better for different types of functions. Here are some common scenarios and the corresponding methods to use:

1. **Direct Substitution**: This method is the simplest and most straightforward. It involves substituting the value that the variable is approaching directly into the function and evaluating the result. Direct substitution works well for functions that are continuous at the given value. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = x^2$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can simply substitute $x = 2$ into the function to get $f(2) = 2^2 = 4$.

2. **Algebraic Manipulation**: As we learned in the previous section, algebraic manipulation can be used to simplify complex expressions and determine the limit of a function. This method is particularly useful when we encounter indeterminate forms, such as $\frac{0}{0}$ or $\frac{\infty}{\infty}$. By applying algebraic properties, such as the sum/difference rule, product rule, quotient rule, and power rule, we can simplify the expression and find the limit. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can simplify the expression using algebraic manipulation to get $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x) = \lim_{{x \to 2}} \frac{(x - 2)(x + 2)}{x - 2} = \lim_{{x \to 2}} (x + 2) = 4$.

3. **Factoring**: Factoring can be a useful method when dealing with rational functions. By factoring the numerator and denominator, we can often cancel out common factors and simplify the expression. This method is particularly helpful when we encounter indeterminate forms like $\frac{0}{0}$ or $\frac{\infty}{\infty}$. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x^2 - 2x}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can factor the numerator and denominator to get $f(x) = \frac{(x - 2)(x + 2)}{x(x - 2)}$. Then, we can cancel out the common factor of $(x - 2)$ to simplify the expression and find the limit.

4. **L'Hôpital's Rule**: L'Hôpital's Rule is a powerful method for finding limits of functions that result in indeterminate forms like $\frac{0}{0}$ or $\frac{\infty}{\infty}$. It involves taking the derivative of the numerator and denominator separately and then evaluating the limit again. This process can be repeated until a determinate form is obtained. L'Hôpital's Rule is particularly useful when dealing with limits involving trigonometric functions or exponential functions. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{\sin(x)}{x}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 0}} f(x)$, we can apply L'Hôpital's Rule by taking the derivative of the numerator and denominator to get $f(x) = \frac{\cos(x)}{1}$. Then, we can evaluate the limit again to find $\lim_{{x \to 0}} f(x) = \cos(0) = 1$.

These are just a few examples of the methods that can be used to find limits. It is important to carefully analyze the given function and choose the appropriate method based on its characteristics. Practice and familiarity with different methods will help you become more proficient in selecting the right procedure to find limits.

### Section: Selecting procedures for determining limits

In the previous section, we learned about algebraic manipulation as a powerful method for determining limits. Now, let's explore how to select the right procedure to find limits based on the given function.

#### Choosing the right method to find limits

When faced with a limit problem, it is important to choose the appropriate method to find the limit. Different methods work better for different types of functions. Here are some common scenarios and the corresponding methods to use:

1. **Direct Substitution**: This method is the simplest and most straightforward. It involves substituting the value that the variable is approaching directly into the function and evaluating the result. Direct substitution works well for functions that are continuous at the given value. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = x^2$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can simply substitute $x = 2$ into the function to get $f(2) = 2^2 = 4$.

2. **Algebraic Manipulation**: As we learned in the previous section, algebraic manipulation can be used to simplify complex expressions and determine the limit of a function. This method is particularly useful when we encounter indeterminate forms, such as $\frac{0}{0}$ or $\frac{\infty}{\infty}$. By applying algebraic properties, such as the sum/difference rule, product rule, quotient rule, and power rule, we can simplify the expression and find the limit. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can simplify the expression using algebraic manipulation to get $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x) = \lim_{{x \to 2}} \frac{(x - 2)(x + 2)}{x - 2} = \lim_{{x \to 2}} (x + 2) = 4$.

3. **Factoring**: Factoring can be a useful method when dealing with rational functions. By factoring the numerator and denominator, we can often cancel out common factors and simplify the expression. This method is particularly helpful when we encounter rational functions with common factors in the numerator and denominator. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x)$, we can factor the numerator as $(x - 2)(x + 2)$ and cancel out the common factor of $(x - 2)$ in the numerator and denominator to get $\lim_{{x \to 2}} f(x) = \lim_{{x \to 2}} (x + 2) = 4$.

4. **Conjugate Method**: The conjugate method is useful when dealing with square roots or complex numbers in the denominator. By multiplying the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator, we can eliminate the square root or complex number and simplify the expression. For example, if we have the function $f(x) = \frac{\sqrt{x} - 2}{x - 4}$ and we need to find $\lim_{{x \to 4}} f(x)$, we can multiply the numerator and denominator by the conjugate of the denominator, which is $\sqrt{x} + 2$, to get $\lim_{{x \to 4}} f(x) = \lim_{{x \to 4}} \frac{(\sqrt{x} - 2)(\sqrt{x} + 2)}{(x - 4)(\sqrt{x} + 2)} = \lim_{{x \to 4}} \frac{x - 4}{(x - 4)(\sqrt{x} + 2)} = \lim_{{x \to 4}} \frac{1}{\sqrt{x} + 2} = \frac{1}{\sqrt{4} + 2} = \frac{1}{4}$.

These are just a few of the methods that can be used to determine limits. It is important to carefully analyze the given function and choose the method that is most appropriate for the situation. Practice problems will be provided in the next subsection to help reinforce these concepts.

### Section: Determining limits using the squeeze theorem

In the previous section, we explored different methods for determining limits, such as direct substitution and algebraic manipulation. Now, let's delve into another powerful technique called the squeeze theorem. The squeeze theorem is a valuable tool for evaluating limits when direct substitution or algebraic manipulation is not applicable.

#### Understanding the squeeze theorem

The squeeze theorem, also known as the sandwich theorem or the pinching theorem, allows us to determine the limit of a function by comparing it to two other functions with known limits. It is particularly useful when dealing with functions that are difficult to evaluate directly.

The squeeze theorem states that if we have three functions, $f(x)$, $g(x)$, and $h(x)$, such that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ in some interval around a particular value $a$, and if $\lim_{{x \to a}} g(x) = \lim_{{x \to a}} h(x) = L$, then $\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) = L$.

In simpler terms, if we can "squeeze" a function $f(x)$ between two other functions $g(x)$ and $h(x)$, and the limits of $g(x)$ and $h(x)$ as $x$ approaches a certain value $a$ are equal, then the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $a$ will also be equal to that common limit.

To apply the squeeze theorem, we need to find two functions that "squeeze" our target function. These two functions should have known limits that are equal. By comparing the target function to these two "squeezing" functions, we can determine the limit of the target function.

Let's look at an example to better understand how the squeeze theorem works:

Example:
Suppose we want to find the limit of the function $f(x) = x^2 \sin\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)$ as $x$ approaches 0.

To apply the squeeze theorem, we need to find two functions, $g(x)$ and $h(x)$, such that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ near 0.

Let's consider the functions $g(x) = -x^2$ and $h(x) = x^2$. It is clear that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ near 0.

Now, let's evaluate the limits of $g(x)$ and $h(x)$ as $x$ approaches 0. We have:

$$\lim_{{x \to 0}} g(x) = \lim_{{x \to 0}} -x^2 = 0$$

$$\lim_{{x \to 0}} h(x) = \lim_{{x \to 0}} x^2 = 0$$

Since $\lim_{{x \to 0}} g(x) = \lim_{{x \to 0}} h(x) = 0$, we can conclude that $\lim_{{x \to 0}} f(x) = 0$ by the squeeze theorem.

The squeeze theorem is a powerful tool that allows us to evaluate limits in situations where other methods may not be applicable. By finding two "squeezing" functions that bound our target function, and ensuring that the limits of these "squeezing" functions are equal, we can confidently determine the limit of the target function.

### Section: Determining limits using the squeeze theorem

In the previous section, we explored different methods for determining limits, such as direct substitution and algebraic manipulation. Now, let's delve into another powerful technique called the squeeze theorem. The squeeze theorem is a valuable tool for evaluating limits when direct substitution or algebraic manipulation is not applicable.

#### Understanding the squeeze theorem

The squeeze theorem, also known as the sandwich theorem or the pinching theorem, allows us to determine the limit of a function by comparing it to two other functions with known limits. It is particularly useful when dealing with functions that are difficult to evaluate directly.

The squeeze theorem states that if we have three functions, $f(x)$, $g(x)$, and $h(x)$, such that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ in some interval around a particular value $a$, and if $\lim_{{x \to a}} g(x) = \lim_{{x \to a}} h(x) = L$, then $\lim_{{x \to a}} f(x) = L$.

In simpler terms, if we can "squeeze" a function $f(x)$ between two other functions $g(x)$ and $h(x)$, and the limits of $g(x)$ and $h(x)$ as $x$ approaches a certain value $a$ are equal, then the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches $a$ will also be equal to that common limit.

To apply the squeeze theorem, we need to find two functions that "squeeze" our target function. These two functions should have known limits that are equal. By comparing the target function to these two "squeezing" functions, we can determine the limit of the target function.

Let's look at an example to better understand how the squeeze theorem works:

Example:
Suppose we want to find the limit of the function $f(x) = x^2 \sin\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)$ as $x$ approaches 0.

To apply the squeeze theorem, we need to find two functions, $g(x)$ and $h(x)$, such that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ near 0.

Let's consider the functions $g(x) = -x^2$ and $h(x) = x^2$. It is clear that $g(x) \leq f(x) \leq h(x)$ for all $x$ near 0. 

Now, let's evaluate the limits of $g(x)$ and $h(x)$ as $x$ approaches 0. 

$$
\lim_{{x \to 0}} g(x) = \lim_{{x \to 0}} (-x^2) = 0
$$

$$
\lim_{{x \to 0}} h(x) = \lim_{{x \to 0}} (x^2) = 0
$$

Since both $g(x)$ and $h(x)$ have a limit of 0 as $x$ approaches 0, we can conclude that the limit of $f(x)$ as $x$ approaches 0 is also 0.

Therefore, using the squeeze theorem, we have determined that:

$$
\lim_{{x \to 0}} f(x) = 0
$$

The squeeze theorem is a powerful tool that allows us to evaluate limits in situations where direct substitution or algebraic manipulation may not be possible. By finding two "squeezing" functions with equal limits and comparing them to our target function, we can confidently determine the limit of the target function.

### Section: Exploring types of discontinuities

In the previous section, we learned about determining limits using the squeeze theorem. Now, let's delve into another important concept in calculus: types of discontinuities. 

#### Understanding discontinuities

A function is said to be continuous at a point if its graph has no breaks, holes, or jumps at that point. However, there are cases where a function may not be continuous at certain points. These points are known as discontinuities.

Discontinuities can occur for various reasons, such as the function having a hole, a jump, or an asymptote. In this section, we will explore the different types of discontinuities and how to identify them.

#### Types of discontinuities

1. **Removable Discontinuity (Hole):** A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, occurs when there is a point on the graph where the function is undefined or has a different value compared to the surrounding points. However, it is possible to redefine the function at that point to make it continuous. This can be done by filling in the hole with a single point or by redefining the function at that point. 

2. **Jump Discontinuity:** A jump discontinuity occurs when the function has a sudden jump or gap in its graph at a particular point. The function approaches different values from the left and right sides of the point, resulting in a visible break in the graph.

3. **Infinite Discontinuity (Vertical Asymptote):** An infinite discontinuity, also known as a vertical asymptote, occurs when the function approaches positive or negative infinity at a particular point. The graph of the function becomes increasingly steep as it approaches the vertical line, but it never crosses or touches it.

4. **Oscillating Discontinuity:** An oscillating discontinuity occurs when the function oscillates or alternates between two or more values as it approaches a particular point. The graph may exhibit a repeating pattern of peaks and valleys, indicating the presence of an oscillating discontinuity.

#### Identifying discontinuities

To identify the type of discontinuity at a given point, we can analyze the behavior of the function as it approaches that point. By examining the left and right limits of the function, we can determine if there is a hole, a jump, an infinite discontinuity, or an oscillating discontinuity.

Let's look at some examples to better understand the different types of discontinuities:

Example 1:
Consider the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - 2}$. At $x = 2$, the denominator becomes zero, resulting in an undefined value. However, if we simplify the function, we find that $f(x) = x + 2$ for all $x$ except $x = 2$. Therefore, the function has a removable discontinuity or a hole at $x = 2$.

Example 2:
Consider the function $g(x) = \begin{cases} 
      x, & x < 0 \\
      1, & x \geq 0 
   \end{cases}$. At $x = 0$, the function jumps from $0$ to $1$, resulting in a jump discontinuity.

Example 3:
Consider the function $h(x) = \frac{1}{x}$. As $x$ approaches $0$ from the left side, $h(x)$ approaches negative infinity, and as $x$ approaches $0$ from the right side, $h(x)$ approaches positive infinity. Therefore, the function has an infinite discontinuity or a vertical asymptote at $x = 0$.

Example 4:
Consider the function $k(x) = \sin\left(\frac{1}{x}\right)$. As $x$ approaches $0$, the function oscillates between $-1$ and $1$, indicating an oscillating discontinuity at $x = 0$.

By understanding the different types of discontinuities and how to identify them, we can gain a deeper insight into the behavior of functions and their graphs. This knowledge is crucial in various areas of calculus, such as analyzing limits and determining the continuity of functions.

### Section: Exploring types of discontinuities

#### Understanding discontinuities

#### Types of discontinuities

1. **Removable Discontinuity (Hole):** A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, occurs when there is a point on the graph where the function is undefined or has a different value compared to the surrounding points. However, it is possible to redefine the function at that point to make it continuous. This can be done by filling in the hole with a single point or by redefining the function at that point. 

2. **Jump Discontinuity:** A jump discontinuity occurs when the function has a sudden jump or gap in its graph at a particular point. The function approaches different values from the left and right sides of the point, resulting in a visible break in the graph.

3. **Infinite Discontinuity (Vertical Asymptote):** An infinite discontinuity, also known as a vertical asymptote, occurs when the function approaches positive or negative infinity at a particular point. #### Identifying discontinuities in functions

Now that we have learned about the different types of discontinuities, let's discuss how to identify them in functions. When analyzing a function, we need to examine its behavior around specific points.

To identify a removable discontinuity or hole, we look for a point where the function is undefined or has a different value compared to the surrounding points. This can be observed by examining the function's equation or graph.

For jump discontinuities, we observe sudden jumps or gaps in the graph of the function at a particular point. The function approaches different values from the left and right sides of the point, resulting in a visible break in the graph.

Infinite discontinuities, or vertical asymptotes, are identified by observing the behavior of the function as it approaches a particular point. If the function approaches positive or negative infinity at that point, without crossing or touching a vertical line, it indicates the presence of an infinite discontinuity.

Lastly, oscillating discontinuities are recognized by the function oscillating or alternating between two or more values as it approaches a specific point. The graph may exhibit a repeating pattern of peaks and valleys, indicating the presence of an oscillating discontinuity.

By understanding and identifying these different types of discontinuities, we can gain a deeper insight into the behavior of functions and their limits. This knowledge is crucial in various areas of calculus and mathematical analysis.

### Section: Defining continuity at a point

In the previous section, we explored different types of discontinuities that can occur in a function. Now, let's focus on understanding continuity at a point.

#### Understanding continuity at a point

Continuity is a fundamental concept in calculus that describes the behavior of a function. A function is said to be continuous at a point if its graph has no breaks, holes, or jumps at that point. In other words, there are no sudden changes or disruptions in the function's values as we approach the point.

To understand continuity at a point, we need to consider three key aspects: the existence of the function at that point, the limit of the function as it approaches the point, and the equality of the function and its limit at that point.

1. **Existence of the function:** For a function to be continuous at a point, it must be defined at that point. In other words, there should be no gaps or undefined values in the function's domain.

2. **Limit of the function:** The limit of a function represents the value that the function approaches as the input approaches a certain point. In the context of continuity, we need to ensure that the limit of the function exists as we approach the point of interest.

3. **Equality of the function and its limit:** Finally, for a function to be continuous at a point, the value of the function at that point must be equal to its limit. This means that the function should not exhibit any sudden jumps or changes in value at that point.

By considering these three aspects, we can determine whether a function is continuous at a specific point. If all three conditions are satisfied, we can conclude that the function is continuous at that point. On the other hand, if any of the conditions are not met, the function is said to be discontinuous at that point.

In the next subsection, we will explore some examples and techniques for determining the continuity of functions at specific points. Understanding continuity is crucial for various applications in calculus, such as finding derivatives and solving equations. So let's dive in and master this important concept!

### Section: Defining continuity at a point

In the previous section, we explored different types of discontinuities that can occur in a function. Now, let's focus on understanding continuity at a point.

#### Understanding continuity at a point

Continuity is a fundamental concept in calculus that describes the behavior of a function. A function is said to be continuous at a point if its graph has no breaks, holes, or jumps at that point. In other words, there are no sudden changes or disruptions in the function's values as we approach the point.

To understand continuity at a point, we need to consider three key aspects: the existence of the function at that point, the limit of the function as it approaches the point, and the equality of the function and its limit at that point.

1. **Existence of the function:** For a function to be continuous at a point, it must be defined at that point. In other words, there should be no gaps or undefined values in the function's domain.

2. **Limit of the function:** The limit of a function represents the value that the function approaches as the input approaches a certain point. In the context of continuity, we need to ensure that the limit of the function exists as we approach the point of interest.

3. **Equality of the function and its limit:** Finally, for a function to be continuous at a point, the value of the function at that point must be equal to its limit. This means that the function should not exhibit any sudden jumps or changes in value at that point.

By considering these three aspects, we can determine whether a function is continuous at a specific point. If all three conditions are satisfied, we can conclude that the function is continuous at that point. On the other hand, if any of the conditions are not met, the function is said to be discontinuous at that point.

Now, let's practice applying these concepts to some problems.

#### Practice problems: Defining continuity at a point

1. Determine whether the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 1}{x - 1}$ is continuous at $x = 1$.

2. Find the value of $k$ that makes the function $g(x) = \begin{cases} 
      kx + 2 & \text{if } x < 2 \\
      3x - 1 & \text{if } x \geq 2 
   \end{cases}$ continuous at $x = 2$.

3. Consider the function $h(x) = \sqrt{x}$. Is $h(x)$ continuous at $x = 0$?

Take your time to solve these problems, and remember to apply the three conditions we discussed earlier to determine the continuity at each point.

### Section: Confirming continuity over an interval

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of continuity at a point. Now, let's extend our understanding to continuity over an interval. 

#### Understanding continuity over an interval

When we talk about continuity over an interval, we are interested in whether a function is continuous for all values within that interval. In other words, we want to confirm that there are no sudden breaks, holes, or jumps in the function's values over the entire interval.

To determine if a function is continuous over an interval, we need to ensure that it satisfies the three key aspects of continuity: the existence of the function, the limit of the function, and the equality of the function and its limit.

1. **Existence of the function:** Just like in continuity at a point, for a function to be continuous over an interval, it must be defined for all values within that interval. We need to make sure that there are no gaps or undefined values in the function's domain over the entire interval.

2. **Limit of the function:** Similarly, we need to consider the limit of the function as it approaches each endpoint of the interval. The limit should exist and be the same from both sides of the endpoint. This ensures that there are no sudden changes or disruptions in the function's behavior as we approach the endpoints.

3. **Equality of the function and its limit:** Finally, for a function to be continuous over an interval, the value of the function at each point within the interval must be equal to its limit at that point. This means that the function should not exhibit any sudden jumps or changes in value over the entire interval.

By considering these three aspects, we can determine whether a function is continuous over a specific interval. If all three conditions are satisfied for every point within the interval, we can conclude that the function is continuous over that interval. On the other hand, if any of the conditions are not met for any point within the interval, the function is said to be discontinuous over that interval.

Now, let's practice applying these concepts to some problems.

#### Practice problems: Confirming continuity over an interval

1. Determine whether the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x}$ is continuous over the interval $(1, 5)$.

2. Determine whether the function $g(x) = \sqrt{x}$ is continuous over the interval $[0, 4]$.

3. Determine whether the function $h(x) = \frac{1}{x-2}$ is continuous over the interval $(-\infty, 2) \cup (2, \infty)$.

Remember to consider the existence of the function, the limit of the function, and the equality of the function and its limit for each problem.

Let's work through these problems step by step to solidify our understanding of continuity over an interval.
