### Section: Removing discontinuities

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of continuity over an interval. Now, let's explore techniques for removing discontinuities in functions.

#### Understanding discontinuities

A discontinuity in a function occurs when there is a sudden break, hole, or jump in the function's values. Discontinuities can make it challenging to analyze and work with functions, so it is often desirable to remove them.

#### Types of discontinuities

There are three main types of discontinuities: removable, jump, and infinite. 

1. **Removable discontinuities:** A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, can be "filled in" by redefining the function at that point. This means that the function can be made continuous by assigning a value to the hole. 

2. **Jump discontinuities:** A jump discontinuity occurs when the function "jumps" from one value to another at a specific point. The function may have different limits from the left and right sides of the point, resulting in a visible gap in the graph.

3. **Infinite discontinuities:** An infinite discontinuity happens when the function approaches positive or negative infinity at a specific point. This can occur when the denominator of a fraction approaches zero, causing the function to become unbounded.

#### Techniques for removing discontinuities

To remove discontinuities in a function, we can employ various techniques depending on the type of discontinuity.

1. **Removing removable discontinuities:** For removable discontinuities, we can redefine the function at the point of discontinuity. By assigning a value to the hole, we can make the function continuous. This involves evaluating the limit of the function as it approaches the point of discontinuity and assigning that value to the function at that point.

2. **Handling jump discontinuities:** Jump discontinuities can be addressed by redefining the function at the point of the jump. We can assign different values to the function from the left and right sides of the point, ensuring that the limits from both sides match. This way, we can eliminate the visible gap in the graph.

3. **Dealing with infinite discontinuities:** Infinite discontinuities can be resolved by simplifying the function or applying algebraic manipulations. By factoring, canceling common factors, or rationalizing the denominator, we can often transform the function into a form that avoids division by zero. This allows us to redefine the function at the point of the infinite discontinuity and make it continuous.

By employing these techniques, we can remove discontinuities in functions and make them continuous over a given interval. This enables us to analyze and work with functions more effectively, providing a clearer understanding of their behavior.

### Section: Removing discontinuities

In the previous section, we discussed the concept of continuity over an interval. Now, let's explore techniques for removing discontinuities in functions.

#### Understanding discontinuities

A discontinuity in a function occurs when there is a sudden break, hole, or jump in the function's values. Discontinuities can make it challenging to analyze and work with functions, so it is often desirable to remove them.

#### Types of discontinuities

There are three main types of discontinuities: removable, jump, and infinite. 

1. **Removable discontinuities:** A removable discontinuity, also known as a hole, can be "filled in" by redefining the function at that point. This means that the function can be made continuous by assigning a value to the hole. 

2. **Jump discontinuities:** A jump discontinuity occurs when the function "jumps" from one value to another at a specific point. The function may have different limits from the left and right sides of the point, resulting in a visible gap in the graph.

3. **Infinite discontinuities:** An infinite discontinuity happens when the function approaches positive or negative infinity at a specific point. This can occur when the denominator of a fraction approaches zero, causing the function to become unbounded.

#### Techniques for removing discontinuities

To remove discontinuities in a function, we can employ various techniques depending on the type of discontinuity.

1. **Removing removable discontinuities:** For removable discontinuities, we can redefine the function at the point of discontinuity. By assigning a value to the hole, we can make the function continuous. This involves evaluating the limit of the function as it approaches the point of discontinuity and assigning that value to the function at that point.

2. **Handling jump discontinuities:** Jump discontinuities can be addressed by redefining the function at the point of the jump. We can assign different values to the function from the left and right sides of the point, ensuring that the function approaches the same value from both directions. This eliminates the visible gap in the graph.

3. **Dealing with infinite discontinuities:** Infinite discontinuities can be resolved by redefining the function at the point where it approaches infinity. This can involve simplifying the function or applying algebraic manipulations to remove the denominator that causes the function to become unbounded.

#### Practice problems: Removing discontinuities

Now, let's practice removing discontinuities in functions. Solve the following problems:

1. Find the value of $a$ that makes the function $f(x) = \frac{x^2 - 4}{x - a}$ continuous at $x = 2$.

2. Determine the values of $a$ and $b$ that remove the jump discontinuity in the function $g(x) = \begin{cases} 
      ax + b & x < 0 \\
      x^2 + 1 & x \geq 0 
   \end{cases}$

3. Remove the infinite discontinuity in the function $h(x) = \frac{3x^2 - 2x + 1}{x^2 - 4}$ by redefining the function at the point where it approaches infinity.

Take your time to solve these problems, and refer back to the techniques discussed earlier to guide you. Good luck!

### Section: Connecting infinite limits and vertical asymptotes

In the previous section, we discussed techniques for removing discontinuities in functions. Now, let's explore the connection between infinite limits and vertical asymptotes.

#### Understanding infinite limits

An infinite limit occurs when the limit of a function approaches positive or negative infinity as the input approaches a certain value. This can happen when the denominator of a fraction approaches zero, causing the function to become unbounded.

For example, consider the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x}$. As $x$ approaches 0 from the right side, the function values become larger and larger, approaching positive infinity. Similarly, as $x$ approaches 0 from the left side, the function values become smaller and smaller, approaching negative infinity.

#### Vertical asymptotes

A vertical asymptote is a vertical line that a graph approaches but never touches. It occurs when the function has an infinite limit as the input approaches a certain value. In other words, a vertical asymptote represents the behavior of the function as it approaches positive or negative infinity.

For example, let's consider the function $f(x) = \frac{1}{x}$. As we mentioned earlier, as $x$ approaches 0, the function values become unbounded. Therefore, the graph of $f(x)$ has a vertical asymptote at $x = 0$.

#### Connection between infinite limits and vertical asymptotes

The connection between infinite limits and vertical asymptotes is straightforward. If a function has an infinite limit as the input approaches a certain value, then there is a vertical asymptote at that value.

In other words, if the function approaches positive or negative infinity as the input approaches a certain value, then the graph of the function will have a vertical asymptote at that value.

Understanding this connection is crucial for analyzing the behavior of functions and their graphs. It allows us to identify vertical asymptotes and predict how the function values behave as the input approaches these asymptotes.

In the next section, we will explore techniques for finding vertical asymptotes and understanding their significance in the context of functions.

### Section: Connecting infinite limits and vertical asymptotes

In the previous section, we discussed techniques for removing discontinuities in functions. Now, let's explore the connection between infinite limits and vertical asymptotes.

#### Understanding infinite limits

### Section: Connecting limits at infinity and horizontal asymptotes

In the previous section, we explored the connection between infinite limits and vertical asymptotes. Now, let's delve into the relationship between limits at infinity and horizontal asymptotes.

#### Understanding limits at infinity

A limit at infinity occurs when the limit of a function approaches a specific value as the input becomes infinitely large or infinitely small. This can happen when the numerator and denominator of a fraction have the same degree, causing the function to approach a constant value.

For example, consider the function $f(x) = \frac{3x^2 + 2x - 1}{2x^2 - x + 5}$. As $x$ becomes infinitely large or infinitely small, the function values approach a constant value, such as 1 or -2. This behavior is indicative of a limit at infinity.

#### Horizontal asymptotes

A horizontal asymptote is a horizontal line that a graph approaches but never crosses. It occurs when the function has a limit at infinity as the input becomes infinitely large or infinitely small. In other words, a horizontal asymptote represents the long-term behavior of the function as it approaches infinity.

For example, let's consider the function $f(x) = \frac{3x^2 + 2x - 1}{2x^2 - x + 5}$. As $x$ becomes infinitely large or infinitely small, the function values approach a constant value, such as 1 or -2. Therefore, the graph of $f(x)$ has a horizontal asymptote at $y = 1$ or $y = -2$.

#### Connection between limits at infinity and horizontal asymptotes

The connection between limits at infinity and horizontal asymptotes is crucial in understanding the behavior of functions and their graphs. If a function has a limit at infinity as the input becomes infinitely large or infinitely small, then there is a horizontal asymptote at the corresponding value.

In other words, if the function approaches a specific value as the input becomes infinitely large or infinitely small, then the graph of the function will have a horizontal asymptote at that value.

Understanding this connection allows us to analyze the long-term behavior of functions and predict how their graphs will approach certain values as $x$ becomes infinitely large or infinitely small.

In the next section, we will explore various techniques for finding limits at infinity and determining horizontal asymptotes. Stay tuned!

### Section: Connecting limits at infinity and horizontal asymptotes

#### Understanding limits at infinity

#### Horizontal asymptotes

A horizontal asymptote is a horizontal line that a graph approaches but never crosses. It occurs when the function has a limit at infinity as the input becomes infinitely large or infinitely small. In other words, a horizontal asymptote represents the long-term behavior of the function as it approaches infinity.

For example, let's consider the function $f(x) = \frac{3x^2 + 2x - 1}{2x^2 - x + 5}$. As $x$ becomes infinitely large or infinitely small, the function values approach a constant value, such as 1 or -2. Therefore, the graph of $f(x)$ has a horizontal asymptote at $y = 1$ or $y = -2$.

### Section: Working with the intermediate value theorem

The intermediate value theorem is a powerful tool in calculus that allows us to determine if a function has a specific value between two given points. It helps us understand the behavior of functions and provides a way to find solutions to equations that cannot be solved algebraically.

#### Understanding the intermediate value theorem

The intermediate value theorem states that if a function is continuous on a closed interval [a, b], and it takes on two different values, say c and d, at the endpoints a and b, then it must also take on every value between c and d at some point within the interval.

In simpler terms, if we have a continuous function that starts at one value and ends at another value, it must pass through every value in between. This theorem is particularly useful when we want to find solutions to equations that cannot be solved algebraically.

Let's consider an example to illustrate the intermediate value theorem. Suppose we have a function f(x) that is continuous on the interval [0, 1] and f(0) = -2 and f(1) = 3. According to the intermediate value theorem, since f(0) = -2 and f(1) = 3, the function must take on every value between -2 and 3 at some point within the interval [0, 1].

This means that there exists at least one value, let's call it c, such that -2 < c < 3 and f(c) = c. In other words, the function f(x) crosses the line y = c at least once between x = 0 and x = 1.

The intermediate value theorem is not only useful for finding solutions to equations, but it also helps us understand the behavior of functions. By analyzing the values of a function at its endpoints and the values it takes on in between, we can gain insights into the overall shape and characteristics of the function.

In the next subsection, we will explore some examples and applications of the intermediate value theorem to further solidify our understanding.

### Section: Working with the intermediate value theorem

The intermediate value theorem is a powerful tool in calculus that allows us to determine if a function has a specific value between two given points. It helps us understand the behavior of functions and provides a way to find solutions to equations that cannot be solved algebraically.

#### Understanding the intermediate value theorem

The intermediate value theorem states that if a function is continuous on a closed interval [a, b], and it takes on two different values, say c and d, at the endpoints a and b, then it must also take on every value between c and d at some point within the interval.

In simpler terms, if we have a continuous function that starts at one value and ends at another value, it must pass through every value in between. This theorem is particularly useful when we want to find solutions to equations that cannot be solved algebraically.

Let's consider an example to illustrate the intermediate value theorem. Suppose we have a function $f(x)$ that is continuous on the interval [0, 1] and $f(0) = -2$ and $f(1) = 3$. According to the intermediate value theorem, since $f(0) = -2$ and $f(1) = 3$, the function must take on every value between -2 and 3 at some point within the interval [0, 1].

This means that there exists at least one value, let's call it c, such that -2 < c < 3 and $f(c) = c$. In other words, the function $f(x)$ crosses the line $y = c$ at least once between $x = 0$ and $x = 1$.

The intermediate value theorem is not only useful for finding solutions to equations, but it also helps us understand the behavior of functions. By analyzing the values of a function at its endpoints and the values it takes on in between, we can gain insights into the overall shape and characteristics of the function.

#### Applying the intermediate value theorem

Now that we understand the intermediate value theorem, let's explore some examples and applications to further solidify our understanding.

##### Example 1: Finding a root of a function

Suppose we have a function $g(x)$ that is continuous on the interval [1, 4] and $g(1) = -5$ and $g(4) = 2$. We want to find a value $c$ such that $g(c) = 0$, in other words, we want to find a root of the function.

According to the intermediate value theorem, since $g(1) = -5$ and $g(4) = 2$, the function must take on every value between -5 and 2 at some point within the interval [1, 4]. Since we are looking for a root, we know that the function must cross the x-axis at least once between $x = 1$ and $x = 4$.

By analyzing the behavior of the function and using the intermediate value theorem, we can conclude that there exists at least one value $c$ such that $g(c) = 0$. We can further approximate the value of $c$ by using numerical methods such as the bisection method or Newton's method.

##### Example 2: Determining the existence of a solution

Consider a function $h(x)$ that is continuous on the interval [-1, 1] and $h(-1) = 2$ and $h(1) = 2$. We want to determine if there exists a solution to the equation $h(x) = 3$ within the interval [-1, 1].

According to the intermediate value theorem, since $h(-1) = 2$ and $h(1) = 2$, the function must take on every value between 2 and 2 at some point within the interval [-1, 1]. However, since the function does not take on the value 3 at any point within the interval, we can conclude that there is no solution to the equation $h(x) = 3$ within the interval [-1, 1].

By applying the intermediate value theorem, we can determine the existence or non-existence of solutions to equations within a given interval.

In the next subsection, we will explore more examples and applications of the intermediate value theorem to further enhance our understanding.

## Chapter: Limits and continuity

### Introduction

Welcome to the chapter on Limits and Continuity in the book "Mastering Precalculus: A Comprehensive Guide." In this chapter, we will delve into the fundamental concepts of limits and continuity, which form the building blocks of calculus.

Limits play a crucial role in understanding the behavior of functions as they approach specific values. By studying limits, we can determine the behavior of functions at points where they are not defined or where they exhibit peculiar characteristics. This knowledge is essential for solving problems involving rates of change, optimization, and approximation.

Continuity, on the other hand, focuses on the smoothness and connectedness of functions. A function is said to be continuous if it has no abrupt jumps, holes, or vertical asymptotes. Understanding continuity is vital for analyzing the behavior of functions over intervals and determining their overall behavior.

Throughout this chapter, we will explore various techniques for evaluating limits, including direct substitution, factoring, and rationalizing. We will also discuss the properties of limits and how they can be used to prove the existence or non-existence of limits.

Additionally, we will examine the concept of continuity and its relationship with limits. We will learn about the different types of discontinuities and how to identify them. We will also explore the Intermediate Value Theorem and its applications in determining the existence of solutions to equations.

By the end of this chapter, you will have a solid understanding of limits and continuity, enabling you to confidently tackle more advanced topics in calculus. So let's dive in and master the art of limits and continuity!

### Section: Working with the intermediate value theorem

The Intermediate Value Theorem is a powerful tool in calculus that allows us to determine the existence of solutions to equations. It states that if a function is continuous on a closed interval [a, b] and takes on two different values, say c and d, then it must also take on every value between c and d.

This theorem is particularly useful when we want to find a solution to an equation but cannot solve it algebraically. Instead, we can use the Intermediate Value Theorem to show that a solution exists within a given interval.

#### Applying the intermediate value theorem

To apply the Intermediate Value Theorem, we follow these steps:

1. Identify the function and the interval on which we want to find a solution.
2. Evaluate the function at the endpoints of the interval to determine the values it takes on.
3. If the function takes on two different values at the endpoints, we can conclude that it must also take on every value in between.

Let's look at an example to see how this works in practice.

Example: Suppose we want to find a solution to the equation $x^3 - 2x + 1 = 0$ on the interval [0, 1].

1. The function we are considering is $f(x) = x^3 - 2x + 1$.
2. Evaluating the function at the endpoints of the interval, we find that $f(0) = 1$ and $f(1) = 0$.
3. Since the function takes on two different values at the endpoints, we can conclude that it must also take on every value in between. Therefore, there exists a solution to the equation $x^3 - 2x + 1 = 0$ on the interval [0, 1].

The Intermediate Value Theorem can be a powerful tool for finding solutions to equations when algebraic methods are not feasible. It allows us to determine the existence of solutions by analyzing the behavior of a function over an interval.

In the next section, we will explore more applications of the Intermediate Value Theorem and learn how to use it effectively in problem-solving.
